The Principle that One Thing Cannot Have Two Independent Causes
The commentator states and proves the principle that a single individual cannot be caused by two independent causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical principle that a single individual cannot be caused by two independent causes and how it withstands a common Ash'ari objection.

Core Idea
One effect cannot have two sufficient causes — a key principle in kalam.

This lesson examines a foundational rule of causality: a single entity cannot be produced by two independent causes. You will read the principle's statement and its defense against a potential objection from Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how the commentator first states the principle, then explains it, and then addresses an objection.
The passage uses technical terms like 'علة مستقلة'.
2
Identify the structure
The passage has four parts: the principle statement, its explanation, the objection from Ash'aris, and two refutations. Mark each part.
The objection is introduced by 'وما قيل'.
3
Reflect on the refutation
The commentator gives two reasons to refute the objection. Understand why the objection is mistaken. Ask yourself: what does 'فاعلية' mean? How is it different from 'علية'?
The key distinction is between agency and causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين ]
( الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين؛ لوجهين : الأول : لو علل ) الواحد
قوله : ( لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين) أي يمتنع أن يجتمع عليه علتان يكون كل منهما كافياً وجوده، وكذا توارد الناقصتين اللتين يستلزم تعدد التامتين كالمادتين والصورتين والفاعلين وما قيل إن هذا الحكم لا يصح عند الأشاعرة؛ لانحصار العلية عندهم في ذاته تعالى فوهم أما أولاً فلان مذهب الأشاعرة انحصار الفاعلية في ذاته تعالى، كما سيجيء في المقصد الثالث ، لا انحصار العلية مطلقاً. وكيف يقول عاقل بعد احتياج الكل إلى الجزء، وعدم احتياج العرض إلى الموضوع. وأما ثانياً فلان الحكم بامتناع اجتماعهما لا يتوقف على وجودهما في الخارج.
[The Second Objective: The one in person is not caused by two independent causes]
(The one in person is not caused by two independent causes; for two reasons: The first: if the one were caused)
His statement: (He is not caused by two independent causes) i.e., it is impossible for two causes, each sufficient for its existence, to come together, and likewise the occurrence of two incomplete causes which necessitate the multiplicity of two complete causes, like two matters, two forms, and two agents. And what was said that this ruling is not valid according to the Ash'aris because causality is confined to His essence, Exalted is He, is mistaken. For first, the Ash'ari school is the confinement of agency (al-fa'iliyya) to His essence, Exalted is He, as will come in the Third Objective, not the confinement of causality absolutely. And how could a rational person say, given the need of the whole for the part, and the non-need of the accident for the subject? And second, because the ruling of the impossibility of their coming together does not depend on their existence in the external.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a statement and proof of a fundamental kalam principle: a single individual (al-wahid bi al-shakhs) cannot be caused by two independent causes ('illatayn mustaqillatayn). The commentator explains that this means it is impossible for two causes, each sufficient for the existence of the effect, to come together, and likewise for two incomplete causes whose conjunction entails two complete causes (like two matters, two forms, or two agents). An objection attributed to the Ash'aris is raised: this ruling is invalid according to them because they restrict causality to God's essence. The commentator refutes this in two ways: First, the Ash'ari school only restricts efficient agency (al-fa'iliyya) to God, not causality in absolute terms; furthermore, one cannot deny the need of the whole for the part or the accident's need for its subject. Second, the ruling of impossibility does not depend on the actual existence of two independent causes in external reality, so the principle holds irrespective of metaphysical commitments.

Key Terms
علة مستقلة
توارد العلل
الأشاعرة
علية
فاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari denial of secondary causality invalidates this principle.
Correction: The principle concerns logical impossibility of two sufficient causes, not empirical occurrence; Ash'aris only deny agency to others, not causality absolutely.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the objection that Ash'aris reject this principle mistaken?
A) Ash'aris only deny efficient agency to others, not causality absolutely.
B) Ash'aris accept the principle in this world but not the afterlife.
C) The objection is based on a misquotation of Ash'ari texts.
D) The principle only applies to material causes, not to God.

Key Takeaway
A single individual cannot be caused by two independent causes; this principle holds regardless of metaphysical disagreements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly treated two things as independent sufficient causes for a good outcome in your life—like your effort and God's mercy—without realizing that one must be the real cause? Which one do you actually rely on when tested?
?
Consider a blessing you attribute partly to your own doing and partly to Allah. Does this principle expose a hidden shirk in your heart? Will you commit to seeing Allah as the sole independent cause and your role as merely a means?
?
When you fail, do you blame yourself or circumstances as if they are independent causes? How does knowing that only Allah is the independent cause free you from despair and push you to turn only to Him?

Detailed Classification of Incomplete Causes
Commentator provides an alternative detailed classification of incomplete causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative detailed classification of the incomplete cause into part and external, with all subdivisions, and how it avoids artificiality.

Core Idea
The incomplete cause is neatly classified by part/external, then by locus, condition, and part type.

The commentator, to avoid artificiality in the earlier classification, offers a clearer taxonomy. The incomplete cause is either a part of the effect or external to it. External causes are further split into locus or non-locus, with the latter including condition and absence of obstacle. Parts themselves are either mental (genus, difference) or external (matter, form). This lesson walks through that tree.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the binary divisions and how each branch is defined.
The passage begins with a general statement about what a thing depends on.
2
Identify the two main divisions
The first split: what a thing depends on for its existence is either a part of it or external to it. Label these as 'part' and 'external'.
3
Trace the subdivisions of external causes
The external is either a locus (subject for accident, receptive locus for substantial form) or not a locus. The non-locus is further divided into efficient cause, final cause, or neither. The 'neither' splits into condition (existential) and absence of obstacle (non-existential).
Note that 'preparer' can be considered under condition or absence of obstacle.
4
Understand the divisions of the part
The part is either mental (genus and specific difference) or external (matter and form). Notice that mental parts are not predicamental, avoiding a problem with non-existents.
5
Reflect on the classification's advantage
Consider why this classification avoids artificiality: it follows natural logical divisions without forcing exceptions.
This is explained in the first commentary line.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولك أن تقول في تفصيل أقسام العلة الناقصة ما يتوقف عليه الشيء في وجوده، إما جزء له أو خارج عنه والثاني إما محل للمعلول، فهو الموضوع بالقياس إلى العرض والمحل القابل بالقياس إلى الصورة الجوهرية وحدها، وإما غير محل له فإما ما منه الوجود أو ما لأجله الوجود أولا هذا ولا ذاك. وحينئذ إما أن يكون وجودياً، وهو الشرط، أو عدمياً وهو عدم المانع، والأول أعني ما يكون جزءاً إما أن يكون جزءاً عقلياً، وهو الجنس والفصل، أو جزءاً خارجياً، وهو المادة والصورة .

قوله : ( ولك أن تقول إلخ) لما كان إدخال بعض أقسام العلة الناقصة في التقسيم السابق محتاجاً إلى تكلف أورد تقسيماً؛ لا شائبة من التكلف فيه .

And you may say in detailing the divisions of the incomplete cause: What a thing depends on for its existence is either a part of it or external to it. The second is either a locus for the effect, and it is the subject (mawdu') in relation to the accident and the receptive locus in relation to substantial form alone, or not a locus for it. Then it is either that from which existence comes, or that for which existence comes, or neither this nor that. Then it is either existential, which is the condition, or non-existential, which is the absence of the obstacle. The first, i.e., what is a part, is either a mental part, which is genus and specific difference, or an external part, which is matter and form.

His statement: (And you may say etc.) Since including some divisions of the incomplete cause in the previous classification required artificiality, he presented a classification with no trace of artificiality in it.
His statement: (To substantial form i.e., the particular form, for it needs matter in its existence, although its absolute concept is a cause for the existence of matter.
His statement: (Alone) i.e., not in relation to the combination of form and matter; for in this consideration it is included in the first division.
His statement: (Either existential etc.) As for the preparer (mu'idd), it is included under condition in one regard and under absence of obstacle in another.

Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification (tafseel) of the incomplete cause (al-illa al-naqisa) provided by the commentator as an alternative to an earlier classification that required artificiality. The classification proceeds as follows: (1) What a thing depends on for its existence is either a part of it or external to it. (2) The external is either a locus (mahall) for the effect — the subject (mawdu') relative to an accident, or the receptive locus relative to substantial form alone — or not a locus. (3) The non-locus is either the efficient cause (ma minhu al-wujud) or the final cause (ma li-ajlihi al-wujud) or neither (condition or absence of obstacle). (4) The latter two are either existential (condition) or non-existential (absence of obstacle). (5) The part is either a mental part (juz' 'aqli) — genus and specific difference — or an external part (juz' khariji) — matter and form. The commentary explains that this classification avoids artificiality; clarifies that 'substantial form alone' refers to particular form; notes that the preparer (mu'idd) can be considered under condition or absence of obstacle; and specifies that mental part does not mean predicamental part, thus avoiding the problem of non-predicamental parts like non-existents.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mental parts (genus and difference) are predicamental parts, i.e., parts that can be predicated.
Correction: Mental parts are parts in mental existence, not predicamental parts. They are not subject to the problem of having parts for non-existents.

Checkpoint

A) They are external parts.
B) They are mental parts.
C) They are conditions.
D) They are obstacles.

Key Takeaway
The incomplete cause is systematically classified into part and external, with further subdivisions that clarify the roles of locus, condition, absence of obstacle, and types of parts, avoiding artificiality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or do you simplistically attribute success solely to your own effort, forgetting the countless external factors (locus, efficient cause, final cause) that were required? Examine one recent success in your life and list the hidden causes from this classification.
?
The 'absence of obstacle' is a cause. What spiritual or moral obstacles have you tolerated that prevent a good action from occurring in your life? Are you actively working to remove them, or do you passively accept their presence? Name one obstacle you can remove today.

The First Branching of Incomplete Causes
Sanusi enumerates the initial division of incomplete causes into part and external, and the subcategories of external causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first major division of incomplete causes: whether the cause is a part of the effect or external to it, and the subcategories of external causes.

Core Idea
Incomplete causes are either parts of the effect or external factors.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of incomplete causes. The author begins by stating that the incomplete cause is a distinct category of cause. He then presents the first dichotomy: what the effect depends on is either a part of itself or something external. From there, external causes are further divided into those that are a locus for the effect and those that are not.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening sentence
Notice that the incomplete cause is said to be 'a separate division' (يعد قسماً برأسه). Why is it separate? The commentary will explain.
This sets the stage for the classification.
2
Identify the first dichotomy
What a thing depends on is either a part of it or external to it. This is the primary split.
3
Explore external causes
External causes are then divided into those that are a locus (محل) for the effect and those that are not. The passage gives examples: subject for accident, receptive locus for substantial form.
Note that 'locus' here means the substrate that receives the effect.
4
Non-locus external causes
Non-locus external causes are further divided into efficient cause (ما منه الوجود), final cause (ما لأجله الوجود), and what is neither (condition and absence of preventer).
This completes the first level of classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is considered a separate division. And you may say in detailing the divisions of the incomplete cause: what the thing depends on for its existence is either a part of it or external to it; the second is either a locus for the effect, so it is the subject in relation to the accident and the receptive locus in relation to the substantial form alone, or not a locus; then it is either that from which existence comes or that for which existence is intended, or neither this nor that. Then it is either existent, which is the condition, or non-existent, which is the absence of the preventer. The first—that which is a part—is either a mental part, which is the genus and differentia, or an external part, which is matter and form. He said: 'He made it among its enumeration'—the pronoun in his saying 'so it is matter' refers to that by which a thing is in potentiality, i.e., the restriction of the division, not to the internal that by which a thing is in potentiality, so it includes the subject, but rather the locus in relation to the substantial form. Similarly, the situation in his saying 'so it is form' because it may be external to the effect as a condition for its existence, like the form of a bed for one who does not say it is a part of the bed. He said: ('And you may say etc.')—since including some divisions of the incomplete cause in the previous division required artificiality, he presented a division without any trace of artificiality. He said: ('to the substantial form') i.e., the determinate one, for it depends in its existence on matter, although its absolute is a cause for the existence of matter. Commentary
This passage is a classification of incomplete causes (العلة الناقصة). It begins by stating that the incomplete cause is a separate division of cause. Then it details the divisions: what a thing depends on for its existence is either a part of it or external to it. The external is either a locus for the effect (like subject for accident or receptive locus for substantial form) or not a locus. If not a locus, it is either that from which existence comes (efficient cause) or that for which existence is intended (final cause), or neither (like condition or absence of preventer). Then further classification: the condition is existent, the absence of preventer is non-existent. The part is either mental (genus and differentia) or external (matter and form). The commentary then explains each point: the pronoun 'it' in 'so it is matter' refers to the restriction of the division, not the internal principle; the locus can be external as a condition; the division avoids artificiality; 'substantial form' means determinate form; 'alone' means not in relation to the composite; the preparer (معد) is included under condition or absence of preventer; 'mental part' means part in mental existence, not predicated part; 'genus and differentia' include what is like them; 'external part' means part in external existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first division of incomplete causes?
A) Mental and external parts
B) Part and external
C) Locus and non-locus
D) Condition and absence of preventer

Key Takeaway
Incomplete causes are fundamentally divided into internal parts and external factors, with external factors further categorized by their relationship to the effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage divides causes into internal parts and external factors. Think of your faith (iman): is your belief an internal part of you or influenced by external factors like the environment and teachers? How does this classification help you understand the role of your own will versus external conditions in strengthening your faith? Be honest: are you taking responsibility for the internal part, or blaming external factors for your spiritual state?
?
The efficient cause is 'that from which existence comes'. When you accomplish something, do you attribute the existence of that achievement to yourself as the efficient cause, or do you see yourself as only an incomplete cause, with Allah as the true creator? Reflect on a recent success: how often does the thought 'Allah made it happen' cross your mind compared to 'I did it'? How can you train yourself to see Allah as the ultimate efficient cause while still valuing your role?

Conditions, Preventers, and the Nature of Parts
Sanusi completes the classification by defining conditions, preventers, and the two types of parts, with explanatory commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between conditions and absence of preventers, and the two types of parts: mental and external.

Core Idea
Conditions are existential, preventers are non-existential; parts can be mental (genus/differentia) or external (matter/form).

Having established the overarching division, the author now delves into the nature of the 'neither' category and the types of parts. He explains that this residual category comprises the condition (شرط) and the absence of the preventer (عدم المانع). Then he turns to the parts, classifying them into mental parts, which are the genus and differentia, and external parts, which are matter and form. The subsequent commentary clarifies potential misunderstandings, especially regarding the mental part not being a predicated part.

Guided Path
1
Examine the 'neither' category
The passage says this category is either existential (condition) or non-existential (absence of preventer). Understand this distinction.
2
Define parts
The first part (الأول) refers to the internal part mentioned earlier. It is divided into mental and external parts.
Mental parts are conceptual; external parts are physical.
3
Identify examples
Mental parts are genus and differentia; external parts are matter and form.
4
Read the commentary
The commentary clarifies that 'mental part' means a part in mental existence, not a predicated part. It also discusses the preparer (معد) as falling under both condition and absence of preventer.
This prevents a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعد قسماً برأسه. ولك أن تقول في تفصيل أقسام العلة الناقصة ما يتوقف عليه الشيء في وجوده، إما جزء له أو خارج عنه والثاني إما محل للمعلول، فهو الموضوع بالقياس إلى العرض والمحل القابل بالقياس إلى الصورة الجوهرية وحدها، وإما غير محل له فإما ما منه الوجود أو ما لأجله الوجود أولا هذا ولا ذاك. وحينئذ إما أن يكون وجودياً، وهو الشرط، أو عدمياً وهو عدم المانع، والأول أعني ما يكون جزءاً إما أن يكون جزءاً عقلياً، وهو الجنس والفصل، أو جزءاً خارجياً، وهو المادة والصورة. قوله : فجعل من عدادها فالضمير في قوله فهو المادة راجع إلى ما به الشيء بالقوة، أعني قيد القسم لا إلى الداخل الذي به الشيء بالقوة فيشمل الموضوع، بل المحل بالنسبة إلى الصورة الجوهرية : وكذا الحال في قوله : فهو الصورة؛ لأنها قد تكون خارجة عن المعلول شرطاً لوجوده كالهيئة السريرية عند من لا يقول بجزئيتها للسرير . قوله : ( ولك أن تقول إلخ) لما كان إدخال بعض أقسام العلة الناقصة في التقسيم السابق محتاجاً إلى تكلف أورد تقسيماً؛ لا شائبة من التكلف فيه . قوله : ( إلى الصورة الجوهرية أي المعينة فإنها محتاجة في وجودها إلى المادة، وإن كان مطلقها علة لوجود المادة . قوله: (وحدها) أي لا بالقياس إلى مجموع الصورة والمادة فإنه بهذا الاعتبار داخل في القسم الأول . قوله : (إما وجودياً إلخ) وأما المعد فهو داخل في الشرط باعتبار، وفي عدم المانع باعتبار . قوله : ( جزءاً عقلياً ) أي جزءاً له في الوجود العقلي، وليس المراد به الجزء المحمول، حتى يرد الإشكال بالأجزاء الغير المحمولة للأمور العدمية. قوله : ( وهو الجنس والفصل وما في حكمه. قوله : ( أو جزءاً خارجياً ) أي جزءاً في الوجود الخارجي.
It is considered a separate division. And you may say in detailing the divisions of the incomplete cause: what the thing depends on for its existence is either a part of it or external to it; the second is either a locus for the effect, so it is the subject in relation to the accident and the receptive locus in relation to the substantial form alone, or not a locus; then it is either that from which existence comes or that for which existence is intended, or neither this nor that. Then it is either existent, which is the condition, or non-existent, which is the absence of the preventer. The first—that which is a part—is either a mental part, which is the genus and differentia, or an external part, which is matter and form. He said: 'He made it among its enumeration'—the pronoun in his saying 'so it is matter' refers to that by which a thing is in potentiality, i.e., the restriction of the division, not to the internal that by which a thing is in potentiality, so it includes the subject, but rather the locus in relation to the substantial form. Similarly, the situation in his saying 'so it is form' because it may be external to the effect as a condition for its existence, like the form of a bed for one who does not say it is a part of the bed. He said: ('And you may say etc.')—since including some divisions of the incomplete cause in the previous division required artificiality, he presented a division without any trace of artificiality. He said: ('to the substantial form') i.e., the determinate one, for it depends in its existence on matter, although its absolute is a cause for the existence of matter. He said: ('alone') i.e., not in relation to the composite of form and matter, for in this respect it falls under the first division. He said: ('either existent etc.') As for the preparer, it falls under the condition in one respect and under the absence of the preventer in another. He said: ('a mental part') i.e., a part in mental existence, not meaning the predicated part, so that the problem of non-predicated parts of non-existent things arises. He said: ('which is the genus and differentia and what is like them. He said: ('or an external part') i.e., a part in external existence.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a classification of incomplete causes (العلة الناقصة). It begins by stating that the incomplete cause is a separate division of cause. Then it details the divisions: what a thing depends on for its existence is either a part of it or external to it. The external is either a locus for the effect (like subject for accident or receptive locus for substantial form) or not a locus. If not a locus, it is either that from which existence comes (efficient cause) or that for which existence is intended (final cause), or neither (like condition or absence of preventer). Then further classification: the condition is existent, the absence of preventer is non-existent. The part is either mental (genus and differentia) or external (matter and form). The commentary then explains each point: the pronoun 'it' in 'so it is matter' refers to the restriction of the division, not the internal principle; the locus can be external as a condition; the division avoids artificiality; 'substantial form' means determinate form; 'alone' means not in relation to the composite; the preparer (معد) is included under condition or absence of preventer; 'mental part' means part in mental existence, not predicated part; 'genus and differentia' include what is like them; 'external part' means part in external existence.

جزء عقلي
جزء خارجي

فصل
مادة
صورة
معد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'mental part' means a part that is predicated of the thing, like a logical predicate.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that 'mental part' means a part in mental existence, not a predicated part (جزء محمول).

Checkpoint

B) Genus and differentia
C) Mental and external

Key Takeaway
Incomplete causes include conditions and absence of preventers as external non-locus factors, and internal parts are either mental (conceptual) or external (physical).

The First Proof: A Single Effect Cannot Have Two Independent Causes
First proof of the principle via contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that a numerically single being cannot be caused by two independent causes, and how it responds to an Ash'ari objection.

Core Idea
A single effect cannot be simultaneously needy and independent of its causes.

The first proof for the principle that a single individual cannot have two independent causes is built on a clear contradiction. If an effect had two causes each sufficient on its own, the effect would be both in need of each cause (by virtue of being caused) and independent of each (because each is sufficient). This lesson unpacks that proof and shows why it holds even within Ash'ari theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the structure: principle statement, proof, then objection and refutation.
2
Identify the two premises
The proof uses two premises: (1) causality implies need, (2) independence implies no need. Both are taken as given.
3
Understand the contradiction
If the effect had two independent causes, it would need each (premise 1) but also not need each (premise 2). This is a contradiction regarding the same thing in the same respect.
The contradiction is about existence itself.
4
Examine the Ash'ari objection and response
The passage addresses the claim that this proof fails for Ash'aris who restrict causality to God. The response clarifies that Ash'aris restrict agency, not all causality, and the impossibility does not depend on actual existence.
This shows the proof is universally valid.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof establishes the principle necessarily: a single effect cannot have two independent causes.
This is a foundational principle in discussions of causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين ] ( الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين؛ لوجهين : الأول : لو علل الواحد بالشخص بمستقلتين لكان محتاجاً إليهما، مع أنه مستغنٍ عنهما بدليل استقلال كل منهما ). قوله : ( لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين) أي يمتنع أن يجتمع عليه علتان يكون كل منهما كافياً وجوده، وكذا توارد الناقصتين اللتين يستلزم تعدد التامتين كالمادتين والصورتين والفاعلين وما قيل إن هذا الحكم لا يصح عند الأشاعرة؛ لانحصار العلية عندهم في ذاته تعالى فوهم أما أولاً فلان مذهب الأشاعرة انحصار الفاعلية في ذاته تعالى، كما سيجيء في المقصد الثالث ، لا انحصار العلية مطلقاً. وكيف يقول عاقل بعد احتياج الكل إلى الجزء، وعدم احتياج العرض إلى الموضوع. وأما ثانياً فلان الحكم بامتناع اجتماعهما لا يتوقف على وجودهما في الخارج. قوله : بالشخص ( بمستقلتين) أي لو اجتمع عليه علتان مستقلتان، (لكان محتاجاً إليهما ) أي قوله : ( الأول إلخ ) خلاصته أن العلية تقتضي الاحتياج إلى كل منهما، والاستقلال عدم بحيث يصح الأمرين الاحتياج، فيلزم اجتماع الاحتياج وعدم الاحتياج لشيء واحد بالقياس إلى شيء واحد، في زمان واحد، من جهة واحدة أعني الوجود وقد عرفت سابقاً أن الاحتياج بديهي التصور، ولو عرف باللفظي.
And the second topic: The individual numerically one is not caused by two independent causes. (The individual numerically one is not caused by two independent causes; for two aspects: the first: if the individual one were caused by two independent causes, it would be in need of both, while it is independent of them due to the independence of each.) He said: ('is not caused by two independent causes') meaning it is impossible that two causes, each sufficient for its existence, come together upon it; likewise the succession of two incomplete causes that entail the multiplicity of two complete causes like two matters, two forms, and two agents. And what was said that this judgment is not valid for the Ash'aris due to the restriction of causality in their view to His essence, Exalted, is a delusion. First, because the Ash'ari school restricts agency (الفاعلية) to His essence, Exalted, as will come in the third topic, not causality absolutely. And how can a rational person say, after the need of the whole for the part and the non-need of the accident for the subject? Second, because the judgment of the impossibility of their conjunction does not depend on their existence in the external. He said: 'the individual' (by two independent causes) i.e., if two independent causes came together upon it, (it would be in need of both). He said: ('the first etc.') its summary is that causality requires need for each of them, and independence is such that both matters of need are possible, so there follows the conjunction of need and non-need for one thing in relation to one thing, in one time, from one aspect, namely existence. And you have known earlier that need is self-evident in conception, even if defined verbally.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage states the key principle: a numerically single individual cannot be caused by two independent causes. It then gives the first proof. The proof is based on contradiction: if a single effect had two independent causes, each sufficient for its existence, then the effect would be in need of each cause (since it is caused by them), yet independent of each (since each is sufficient alone). This leads to a contradiction of need and independence simultaneously for the same thing in the same respect. The commentary explains the meaning in detail: 'independent' means each cause is sufficient for the existence of the effect. It also addresses a potential objection that this principle does not hold for Ash'aris because they limit causality to God. The commentary refutes this on two grounds: (1) Ash'aris restrict agency (الفاعلية) to God, not causality in general (since they acknowledge, e.g., the need of the whole for the part and the accident for the subject). (2) The impossibility of two independent causes does not depend on their actual existence in the external. The proof is then restated: causality entails need for each cause, independence entails no need, so contradiction arises regarding the same existence.

Key Terms
واحد بالشخص
علة مستقلة
استقلال
احتياج
علة تامة
فاعلية
علية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This principle does not apply to Ash'ari theology because they hold that only God is a true cause.
Correction: Ash'aris restrict agency (الفاعلية) to God, not causality in general. They acknowledge other causal dependencies (e.g., part-whole, accident-subject). Moreover, the impossibility is a logical judgment independent of actual existence.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the first proof derive?
A) The effect is eternal and temporal
B) The effect is both needy and independent of the same cause
C) The causes are both necessary and contingent
D) The effect is both one and many

Key Takeaway
A single effect cannot have two independent causes because that would require it to be simultaneously needy and independent of each cause, an impossible contradiction.

?

The Meaning of Need: Objection and Answer
Presents an objection about the definition of need and responds to it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible interpretations of 'need' (احتياج) in causality and how the answer resolves the objection by identifying the effect's true causal dependence.

Core Idea
True need means the effect cannot exist without its cause, not mere dependence.

This lesson presents an objection to the definition of need and its answer. The objection tries to limit need to either specific creation or mere dependence, but the answer shows that genuine need requires exclusive dependence on a single cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage through once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection (قيل) followed by an answer (والجواب).
The answer begins with 'والجواب عنه'.
2
Identify the two interpretations of need
The objection splits need into two possible meanings. Find both in the text: (1) needing a specific cause to create it, (2) mere dependence that can be satisfied by any cause. Note the objection's claim that neither forces a unique cause.
3
Understand the answer's logic
The answer argues that the effect depends only on that without which it cannot be realized. If two causes were each necessary, the real cause would be their common factor, not either individually.
This shows that true need excludes independence from the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : هو أن لا يمكن حصول شيء بدون شيء آخر، فما قيل فيه بحث لأنه إن أريد بالاحتياج كونه بحيث لا يمكن وجوده إلا بإيجادها بخصوصها إياه، فلا نسلم أن العلة يجب أن تكون كذلك، وإن أريد به مجرد الاستناد المصحح للفاء، فلا ينافي الاستغناء عنه بغيره. والجواب عنه : أن المعلول لا يستند إلا إلى ما لا يتحقق إلا به فلو كان كل واحد من استناد المعلول إليه كان العلة في الحقيقة هي القدر المشترك بينهما، لا شيء منهما بخصوصه. وحينئذ يمكن اختيار كل من شقي الترديد، ولا يخفى تقريره. ثم قال : ويظهر لك مما قررنا أن توارد العلتين على معلول شخصي محال مطلقاً، سواء كان على سبيل الاجتماع، أو على سبيل التعاقب أو على سبيل البدل وإن ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره في جواب لايقال، مندفع مما يقضي منه العجب، أما أولاً فلان ترديد الاحتياج في المعنيين غير حاصر؛ لما عرفت في معنى الاحتياج، بل غير صحيح لأن المعنى الأول مختص بالفاعل المستقل الذي لا يمكن أن يكون غيره فاعلاً، والمعنى الثاني معنى التقدم الذاتي، وأما ثانياً : فلأن المعلول مستند إلى كل علله الناقصة، إذ واحدة من للاستناد إلا توقف الوجود عليه فكيف يصح أن المعلول لا يستند إلا إلى ما لا يتحقق إلا به؟ ولو كان كذلك لكان قولهم : الواحد الشخصي لا يعلل بعلتين لغواً من الكلام. وأما ثالثاً : فلأنا لا نسلم أنه لو كان كل واحد من الأمرين بحيث المعلول إليه كان العلة في الحقيقة القدر المشترك بينهما ، لا شيء منهما بخصوصه، وهل النزاع إلا فيه.
It was said: Need is that a thing cannot be obtained without another thing. What was said about it is debatable, because if by need is meant that its existence cannot be except by their particular creation of it, then we do not concede that the cause must be like that; and if by it is meant mere dependence that justifies the فاء, then it does not conflict with being independent of it by another. The answer to it is that the effect does not depend except on that without which it is not realized. So if each of the two things were such that the effect depends on it, then the cause in reality would be the common factor between them, not one of them in particular. In that case, either side of the disjunction can be chosen, and its explanation is not hidden. Then he said: It becomes clear to you from what we have established that the succession of two causes upon a single effect is absolutely impossible, whether it be in the manner of conjunction, succession, or substitution. And what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned in answer to 'it is not said' is refuted by what is astonishing. First, because restricting need to the two meanings is not exhaustive; as you know the meaning of need, rather it is not correct, for the first meaning is specific to the independent agent that cannot be otherwise, and the second meaning is that of essential priority. Second, because the effect depends on all its incomplete causes, since not one of them is valid for dependence except that existence depends on it, so how can it be correct that the effect depends only on that without which it is not realized? If it were so, then their statement 'the individual one is not caused by two causes' would be idle talk. Third, because we do not concede that if each of the two were such that the effect depends on it, then the cause in reality would be the common factor between them, not one of them in particular. Is the dispute not about that?
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (قيل) and its answer. The objection concerns the definition of 'need' (احتياج). The objector says need means that a thing cannot be obtained without another; but if need means that the effect's existence depends on the cause's specific creation, we do not accept that a cause must be like that; if it means mere dependence that justifies saying 'because of it', then independence from another is not contradicted. The answer: The effect depends only on that without which it does not exist; if both causes are such, then the real cause is the common factor, not either individually. Then follows a concluding remark: it becomes clear that the succession of two causes on one effect is absolutely impossible, whether simultaneously, successively, or alternatively. Then a rebuttal of another commentator's point: first, the disjunction of need into two meanings is incomplete; second, the effect depends on all its incomplete causes—so how can it depend only on that without which it is not realized? Third, we do not concede that if each cause is necessary, the real cause would be the common factor. The passage ends by pointing out that the dispute is precisely about that.

Key Terms
احتياج
استناد
فاعل مستقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Need is merely dependence that can be fulfilled by any cause.
Correction: True need requires that the effect cannot be realized without that specific cause; otherwise, the cause is not truly necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the real cause if each of two independent things is such that the effect depends on it?
A) The first cause
B) The second cause
C) The common factor between them
D) The effect itself

Key Takeaway
Need (احتياج) in strict causality means the effect cannot exist unless its specific cause exists, which excludes the possibility of independence from that cause.

Or do you subtly attribute it to your own efforts? What would change if you lived with the conviction that every effect needs its specific cause – and that Cause is Allah?

Impossibility of Two Causes on One Effect and Rebuttal
Concludes impossibility of two causes on one effect and rebuts a commentator's view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two independent causes cannot produce the same effect and how the commentator's alternative view is refuted through three arguments.

Core Idea
A single effect cannot originate from two distinct causes — the real cause is their common factor.

This lesson extends the discussion of need to the principle of causal uniqueness. It affirms that two causes cannot concur on one effect, then refutes a commentator who offered a different analysis, providing three specific rebuttals.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding remark and rebuttal
Read the Arabic passage from 'ثم قال' to the end. Notice the clear structure: a claim about the impossibility of two causes, followed by three numbered objections to the commentator.
The commentator's view is introduced with 'وإن ما ذكره الشارح...'.
2
Understand the impossibility claim
The passage states that two causes cannot succeed upon a single effect, whether simultaneously, successively, or by substitution. Grasp the absolute nature of this claim.
This is derived from the earlier answer about need.
3
Analyze the three rebuttals
The passage gives three reasons against the commentator's position. Identify each: (1) the disjunction of need is not exhaustive, (2) the effect depends on all its incomplete causes, (3) the common factor point is not conceded.
The third rebuttal directly challenges the commentator's assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : هو أن لا يمكن حصول شيء بدون شيء آخر، فما قيل فيه بحث لأنه إن أريد بالاحتياج كونه بحيث لا يمكن وجوده إلا بإيجادها بخصوصها إياه، فلا نسلم أن العلة يجب أن تكون كذلك، وإن أريد به مجرد الاستناد المصحح للفاء، فلا ينافي الاستغناء عنه بغيره. والجواب عنه : أن المعلول لا يستند إلا إلى ما لا يتحقق إلا به فلو كان كل واحد من استناد المعلول إليه كان العلة في الحقيقة هي القدر المشترك بينهما، لا شيء منهما بخصوصه. وحينئذ يمكن اختيار كل من شقي الترديد، ولا يخفى تقريره. ثم قال : ويظهر لك مما قررنا أن توارد العلتين على معلول شخصي محال مطلقاً، سواء كان على سبيل الاجتماع، أو على سبيل التعاقب أو على سبيل البدل وإن ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره في جواب لايقال، مندفع مما يقضي منه العجب، أما أولاً فلان ترديد الاحتياج في المعنيين غير حاصر؛ لما عرفت في معنى الاحتياج، بل غير صحيح لأن المعنى الأول مختص بالفاعل المستقل الذي لا يمكن أن يكون غيره فاعلاً، والمعنى الثاني معنى التقدم الذاتي، وأما ثانياً : فلأن المعلول مستند إلى كل علله الناقصة، إذ واحدة من للاستناد إلا توقف الوجود عليه فكيف يصح أن المعلول لا يستند إلا إلى ما لا يتحقق إلا به؟ ولو كان كذلك لكان قولهم : الواحد الشخصي لا يعلل بعلتين لغواً من الكلام. وأما ثالثاً : فلأنا لا نسلم أنه لو كان كل واحد من الأمرين بحيث المعلول إليه كان العلة في الحقيقة القدر المشترك بينهما ، لا شيء منهما بخصوصه، وهل النزاع إلا فيه.
It was said: Need is that a thing cannot be obtained without another thing. What was said about it is debatable, because if by need is meant that its existence cannot be except by their particular creation of it, then we do not concede that the cause must be like that; and if by it is meant mere dependence that justifies the فاء, then it does not conflict with being independent of it by another. The answer to it is that the effect does not depend except on that without which it is not realized. So if each of the two things were such that the effect depends on it, then the cause in reality would be the common factor between them, not one of them in particular. In that case, either side of the disjunction can be chosen, and its explanation is not hidden. Then he said: It becomes clear to you from what we have established that the succession of two causes upon a single effect is absolutely impossible, whether it be in the manner of conjunction, succession, or substitution. And what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned in answer to 'it is not said' is refuted by what is astonishing. First, because restricting need to the two meanings is not exhaustive; as you know the meaning of need, rather it is not correct, for the first meaning is specific to the independent agent that cannot be otherwise, and the second meaning is that of essential priority. Second, because the effect depends on all its incomplete causes, since not one of them is valid for dependence except that existence depends on it, so how can it be correct that the effect depends only on that without which it is not realized? If it were so, then their statement 'the individual one is not caused by two causes' would be idle talk. Third, because we do not concede that if each of the two were such that the effect depends on it, then the cause in reality would be the common factor between them, not one of them in particular. Is the dispute not about that?
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (قيل) and its answer. The objection concerns the definition of 'need' (احتياج). The objector says need means that a thing cannot be obtained without another; but if need means that the effect's existence depends on the cause's specific creation, we do not accept that a cause must be like that; if it means mere dependence that justifies saying 'because of it', then independence from another is not contradicted. The answer: The effect depends only on that without which it does not exist; if both causes are such, then the real cause is the common factor, not either individually. Then follows a concluding remark: it becomes clear that the succession of two causes on one effect is absolutely impossible, whether simultaneously, successively, or alternatively. Then a rebuttal of another commentator's point: first, the disjunction of need into two meanings is incomplete; second, the effect depends on all its incomplete causes—so how can it depend only on that without which it is not realized? Third, we do not concede that if each cause is necessary, the real cause would be the common factor. The passage ends by pointing out that the dispute is precisely about that.

Misconception: Two partial causes can combine to produce one effect such that each is truly a cause of the effect.
Correction: If each is truly necessary, then the real cause is their common factor, but this leads to contradictions; thus, two independent causes cannot produce one individual effect.

Two Meanings of Independent Cause
Explains the two possible meanings of 'independent cause'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possible meanings of 'independent cause' (علة مستقلة) in Islamic theology and why a verbal quibble arises.

Core Idea
The term 'independent cause' can mean either an independently efficient cause or a complete cause.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies what is meant by 'independent cause' in the context of two causes coming together. He presents two distinct interpretations that are often conflated. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments about causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author first states the two meanings explicitly, then addresses a potential objection.
The passage is cut off at the end, so focus on the part we have.
2
Identify the two meanings
Look for the two definitions: 'independently efficient' (المستقل بالتأثير) and 'complete cause' (العلة التامة). Understand how each is derived from the context.
The commentator says the meaning is either one or the other.
3
Understand the verbal quibble
Read the objection: calling each of two causes that are together a 'complete cause' is incorrect. The reply says it's a verbal quibble—the real question is different.
This shows the importance of precise terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أي لو اجتمع عليه علتان (مستقلتان وجه التفسير التنصيص على المراد، ورفع إيهام العبارة عدم جواز التعليل بمستقلتين ولو على سبيل التوارد ثم المراد بالعلة المستقلة، إما المستقل بالتأثير، كما سبق الفهم من العبارات الواقعة في الاستدلال كتأثير إحداهما أو كلتاهما فيه، وكونه أثراً لهما. وإما العلة التامة كما يشعر به كلام الشارح في تقرير الوجه الثاني فإطلاق التأثير مجاز، بناء على أن العلة التامة مؤثرة بما فيها والاستدلال على هذه الدعوى لا يدل على عدم اعتبار دخول المادة والصورة في العلة التي ثبت عدم جواز ،تعددها، بناء على كونها حينئذ ضرورية غير مبرهن عليها كما ظن، لأن كون هذه المادة والصورة مع أمور مخصوصة مؤثرة في المعلول المخصوص بالفعل استقلالاً بما فيها، لا يفيد ضرورة عدم كونهما مع أمور مخصوصة أخر كذلك، فإن قلت : إطلاق العلة التامة على كل من العلتين المستقلتين المجتمعتين لا يكاد يصح لأنهما جملة ما يتوقف عليه الشيء ولا توقف للمعلول على شيء منهما بخصوصه. قلت : هذه مناقشة لفظية، والمقصود أنه هل يجوز أن يجتمع علتان كل منهما يكفي في وجود
He said: i.e., if two independent causes come together upon it. The interpretation is the specification of the intended meaning and removal of the ambiguity of the statement: the impermissibility of causing by two independent causes even in the manner of succession. Then the intended meaning of 'independent cause' is either the independently efficient cause, as understood from the expressions occurring in the proof such as the influence of one or both of them in it, and its being an effect of them; or the complete cause, as indicated by the commentator's speech in the exposition of the second aspect. Then the application of 'influence' is metaphorical, based on the fact that the complete cause influences by what it includes. And the proof of this claim does not indicate that the inclusion of matter and form in the cause whose multiplicity is proven impossible is not considered, based on the assumption that they are then necessary without proof as is thought, because the fact that this matter and form with particular things influence the particular effect actually independently by what they include does not imply the necessity that they are not similarly with other particular things. For if you say: applying 'complete cause' to each of the two independent causes that are together is hardly correct, because they are the sum of what the thing depends on, and the effect does not depend on either of them in particular. I say: this is a verbal quibble. The point is whether two causes, each sufficient for existence, may come together.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'two independent causes' (علتان مستقلتان). It clarifies the meaning: the intended meaning is either (1) the independently efficient cause (المستقل بالتأثير), which is understood from the proof language (e.g., 'influence of one or both'), or (2) the complete cause (العلة التامة), as implied by the commentator in the exposition of the second aspect. If it means the complete cause, then 'influence' is metaphorical because the complete cause influences by including all conditions. The passage then argues that the proof does not deny the inclusion of matter and form in the cause; they are necessary but not proven. The fact that a specific matter and form with particular conditions influence a specific effect does not prove that they cannot be combined with other conditions. An objection is raised: calling each of two causes that are together 'complete cause' is incorrect because the complete cause is the total sum, and the effect does not depend on each individually. The reply is that this is a verbal quibble; the real question is whether two causes each sufficient for existence can come together. The passage is cut off here.

The Principle of One Effect from One Cause
Al-Iji states the principle and proves it by contradiction, addressing an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single individual effect cannot be produced by two independent causes and how the argument resolves an objection about the source of need.

Core Idea
A single effect cannot need and not need the same thing at once, even if the causes differ.

In this passage, al-Iji presents a foundational principle of kalam: one effect cannot have two independent causes. He proves this by showing that it leads to a contradiction. He then anticipates an objection that might try to separate the sources of need and lack of need, and he refutes it decisively. This principle is crucial for understanding divine causality and the uniqueness of God's creative power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the flow from principle to proof to objection to refutation.
The passage is dense; pay attention to the key terms.
2
Understand the principle and its proof
Identify the principle: a single individual cannot be caused by two independent causes. Follow the proof: if two independent causes combine, the effect would need each (due to causality) and not need them (due to independence), a contradiction.
The contradiction is in the same respect (existence).
3
Examine the objection
The objection claims that need and lack of need come from different sources (one cause vs the other), so no actual contradiction. Understand how this tries to avoid the problem.
4
Analyze the refutation
Al-Iji replies that need and lack of need for existence are contradictory regardless of their source. They cannot coexist, whether from one cause or two.
This reinforces the impossibility.
5
Conclude the argument
The coming together of two independent causes entails an impossible contradiction, so its possibility is also impossible. Thus the principle is proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين. أي لو اجتمع عليه علتان مستقلتان، لكان محتاجاً إليهما. والعلية تقتضي الاحتياج إلى كل منهما، والاستقلال عدم الاحتياج، فيلزم اجتماع الاحتياج وعدم الاحتياج لشيء واحد بالقياس إلى شيء واحد، في زمان واحد، من جهة واحدة أعني الوجود. لا يقال منشأ الاحتياج إلى كل واحدة هو عليتها له، ومنشأ عدم الاحتياج إليها علية الأخرى له فلا استحالة في اجتماعهما؛ لأنا نقول : احتياج شيء إلى آخر في وجوده وعدم احتياجه إليه فيه متناقضان، فلا يجتمعان، سواء كانا مستندين إلى سبب واحد أو إلى سببين. واجتماع علتين مستقلتين على معلول واحد شخصي مستلزم لوقوع المحال فيكون إمكان اجتماعهما مستلزماً لإمكانه، وهو أيضاً محال.
The Fifth Observatory - The Second Objective: The one as an individual is not caused by two independent causes. That is, if two independent causes come together upon it, it would be in need of both. Causality requires need for each, and independence is lack of need, so there follows the coexistence of need and lack of need for one thing in relation to one thing, in one time, from one aspect, namely existence. It cannot be said: the source of need for each is its causality for it, and the source of lack of need for it is the causality of the other for it, so there is no impossibility in their coming together; because we say: the need of a thing for another in its existence and its lack of need for it are contradictory, so they do not come together, whether they are based on one cause or two causes. And the coming together of two independent causes upon one individual effect entails the occurrence of the impossible, so the possibility of their coming together entails the possibility of that impossible, and that is also impossible.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the core kalam principle that a single individual cannot be caused by two independent causes. The author first states the principle, then explains why: if two independent causes combined, the effect would need each cause (because it depends on each) yet also not need them (because each is independent and sufficient alone), a contradiction. The objection tries to resolve this by saying the need and lack of need have different sources—need comes from one cause's causality, lack of need from the other's—so no contradiction. The answer firmly refutes this: need and lack of need for the same existence are contradictions regardless of their source; they cannot both hold. Finally, the author notes that the very supposition of such a combination entails an impossible contradiction, so its possibility is also impossible, proving the principle.

Key Terms
علة مستقلة
احتياج
استغناء
معلول شخصي
اجتماع
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Need and lack of need can coexist if they arise from different causes.
Correction: The passage shows that need and lack of need for the same existence are contradictory regardless of their source; they cannot both hold for the same effect in the same respect.

Checkpoint

A) Because the effect would have two different existences.
B) Because the effect would need each cause (due to causality) but also not need them (due to independence), a contradiction in the same respect.
C) Because causes must be simple and cannot be multiple.
D) Because the effect would be dependent on nothing.

Key Takeaway
A single individual effect cannot be produced by two independent causes because that would force a contradiction in the same respect (existence) that cannot be resolved by attributing need and lack of need to different sources.

Why a Single Effect Cannot Have Two Independent Causes
Proving that a single effect cannot have two independent causes and refuting an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is rationally impossible for one effect to have two fully independent causes and how the objection based on different aspects is refuted.

Core Idea
One effect cannot depend on two independent causes without contradiction.

This lesson examines a key argument in causality: how can one thing have more than one cause? The passage explores the logical impossibility of a single effect being caused by two independent causes, and addresses a sophisticated objection. It demonstrates the rigor of kalam reasoning about necessary relationships.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic source and its English translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Pay attention to the flow from principle to proof to objection and refutation.
The text includes explanatory glosses in parentheses; these are part of the commentary tradition.
2
Understand the core claim
Identify the main thesis: a single individual effect cannot be caused by two independent causes each sufficient by itself. Consider why this is stated as a universal rule.
3
Follow the proof
Work through the logical argument: each cause independently can produce the effect, so the effect would exist with each even without the other. This leads to the effect being simultaneously dependent on each cause (since it is caused) and independent of each (since it would exist without it). Contradiction.
4
Examine the objection and refutation
The objection claims that need comes from one cause and non-need from the other, so no contradiction. The refutation states that need and non-need in existence are absolute opposites; they cannot coexist regardless of source. Understand why the aspect-based distinction fails.
The refutation emphasizes that the contradiction is in the same thing essentially, not by aspect.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the conjunction of two independent causes on one effect is impossible, and even the possibility of that conjunction is impossible. Consider the broader implications for causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين إلى كل واحدة منهما . ( للعلية ) أي لكون كل واحد علة له فإن المعلول محتاج إلى علته البتة مستغنياً عنهما ) أي عن كل واحدة منهما ؛ إذ بالنظر إلى كل واحد منهما) أي كل واحد من الأمرين المستقلين بالعلية ( يوجد ) ذلك المعلول الشخصي، ( ولو لم يوجد ) الأمر (الآخر) إذ الفرض أن كل واحد مستقل. (وهو) أي جواز وجوده بكل منهما في زمان واحد وإن لم يوجد الآخر ( معنى الاستغناء ) أي استغناء ذلك المعلول عن الآخر، فيلزم أن يكون محتاجاً إلى كل واحدة من المستقلتين، وغير محتاج إليهما . لا يقال منشأ الاحتياج إلى كل واحدة هو عليتها له، ومنشأ عدم الاحتياج إليها علية الأخرى ،له فلا استحالة في اجتماعهما ؛ لأنا نقول : احتياج شيء إلى آخر في وجوده وعدم احتياجه إليه فيه متناقضان، فلا يجتمعان، سواء كانا مستندين إلى سبب واحد أو إلى سببين واجتماع علتين مستقلتين على معلول واحد شخصي مستلزم لوقوع المحال فيكون إمكان اجتماعهما مستلزماً لإمكانه، وهو أيضاً محال . قوله : ( فلا استحالة في اجتماعهما لاختلاف جهتي الاحتياج وعدمه. قوله : ( لأنا نقول إلخ يعني أن الاحتياج وعدمه فيما نحن فيه غير مقيد بجهة وحيثية، حتى توجب تغاير محلهما بالاعتبار، بل مطلق إنما التعدد والاختلاف في السبب، فيلزم فيما نحن فيه اجتماع الاحتياج وعدمه في شيء واحد بالذات والاعتبار، وإن كان سببهما متعدداً. قوله : ( وهو أيضاً محال أي إمكان المحال أيضاً محال فيمتنع اجتماع العلتين على معلول واحد شخصي، وهو المطلوب.
Fifth observation - Second topic: The individual thing is not caused by two independent causes reaching each one of them. (For causality) i.e., because each one is a cause of it: for the effect is necessarily in need of its cause, independent of both of them) i.e., of each one of them; for with respect to each one of them) i.e., each one of the two things independent in causality (exists) that individual effect, (even if the other) thing (does not exist) because the assumption is that each one is independent. (And it) i.e., the permissibility of its existence by each one of them at one time even if the other does not exist (is the meaning of independence) i.e., the independence of that effect from the other. So it follows that it is in need of each one of the two independent causes, and not in need of them both. It cannot be said: the source of need for each one is its causality for it, and the source of non-need for it is the causality of the other for it, so there is no impossibility in their conjunction; for we say: need of something for another in its existence and non-need for it in it are contradictory, so they cannot coexist, whether they are based on one cause or two causes. And the conjunction of two independent causes upon one individual effect entails the occurrence of the impossible, so the possibility of their conjunction entails the possibility of it (the impossible), and that is also impossible. His saying: 'So there is no impossibility in their conjunction due to the difference of the aspects of need and non-need.' His saying: 'For we say, etc.' means that need and non-need in our case are not restricted by aspect and modality, so as to necessitate the difference of their locus by consideration, but are absolute; rather, multiplicity and difference are in the cause, so in our case there is conjunction of need and non-need in one thing essentially and by consideration, even if their causes are multiple. His saying: 'And it is also impossible' means the possibility of the impossible is also impossible, so the conjunction of the two causes upon one individual effect is impossible, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation of an objection regarding the impossibility of a single effect having two independent causes. The author begins by stating the principle: a single individual effect cannot be caused by two independent causes. He explains that if two independent causes each fully cause the same effect, then with respect to each cause the effect exists even if the other cause does not. This implies that the effect is both in need of each cause (since it is caused) and independent of each cause (since it can exist without the other). The effect would thus be simultaneously in need of each cause and not in need of each cause. An objector argues that the need comes from one cause and the lack of need from the other, so there is no contradiction. The author refutes this by stating that need and non-need in existence are absolute contradictions; they cannot coexist in the same thing irrespective of the source. Therefore, two independent causes cannot conjointly cause one effect. The commentary further explains that the objection fails because the need and non-need are not qualified by aspect; they recur to the same thing essentially. Thus, the possibility of such conjunction would imply the possibility of an impossibility, which is itself impossible.

Key Terms
علتين مستقلتين
معلول شخصي
احتياج
استغناء
علة
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That need and non-need from different sources can coexist without contradiction because they are based on different aspects.
Correction: Need and non-need in existence are absolute opposites; they cannot be reconciled by attributing them to different causes or aspects. The contradiction remains in the same thing essentially.

Checkpoint
Why does the conjunction of two independent causes on one effect entail impossibility?
A) Because the effect would exist twice.
B) Because the effect would be both in need of each cause and not in need of each cause, which is contradictory.
C) Because two causes cannot exist simultaneously.
D) Because the effect would have two different essences.

Key Takeaway
A single effect cannot be caused by two independent causes because it would be simultaneously dependent on and independent of each cause, an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing, do you ever attribute it to multiple independent sources—your effort, luck, or other people—rather than solely to Allah? How does this lesson's logical proof that a single effect cannot have two independent causes challenge you to purify your tawheed and give all credit to Allah alone?
?
In times of hardship, do you subconsciously rely on independent 'saviors'—money, relationships, or plans—as backup causes alongside Allah? Realize that this lesson proves such independence is impossible; how does that compel you to place your entire trust in Allah without dividing your reliance?
?
Consider a situation where you felt your success was due to both your own planning and Allah's decree. Did you treat them as two independent causes for the same outcome? This lesson refutes that. In what specific way will you adjust your mindset to avoid this logical and theological error, ensuring you see Allah as the sole and direct cause?

Causality and Created Things

Two Arguments Against Alternate Causation
Presents two logical pitfalls of alternate causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two causes cannot alternately produce a single effect, through the arguments of re-creation of the non-existent and attainment of the attained.

Core Idea
Alternate causation leads to either re-creating the non-existent or duplicating the attained.

Theologians argue that even if two causes act one after the other, they cannot both be causes of the same individual effect. The passage establishes this by showing that any scenario leads to a logical contradiction. We will explore the two main pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the two cases presented.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the conditional structure.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find where the passage describes the first case (effect ceases) and the second case (effect persists). Each leads to an impossibility.
The first is 'iʿādat al-maʿdūm', the second is 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil'.
3
Reflect on why these are impossible
Consider why re-creating the non-existent is logically impossible, and why attaining what is already attained is redundant. This shows alternate causation is untenable.
These are foundational principles in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تواردهما على سبيل البدل مع امتناع الاجتماع، إذا لم يمكن تعاقبهما فلا استحالة فيه بأن تكون كل واحدة منهما بحيث لو وجدت ابتداء وجد ذلك المعلول الشخصي، فإذا وجدت إحداهما وجد المعلول، وامتنع حينئذ وجود الأخرى؛ إذ لو أمكن أن تعدم الأولى، وتوجد الأخرى فإن عدم المعلول بعدم الأولى، ووجد بإيجاد الثانية، لزم إعادة المعدوم. وإن لم يعدم وجب أن تكون الثانية مفيدة للمعلول أصل وجوده الحاصل له بإيجاد الأولى فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل. ولا يمكن أن يكون وجود المعلول من كل منهما ولو بما فيهما. وتفسير العلة التامة بجملة ما يتوقف عليه الشيء بناء على ما تقرر عندهم من عدم جواز تعددها. قوله : ( إذا لم يمكن إلخ يفهم منه أنه إذا أمكن تعاقبهما يستحيل تواردهما على سبيل البدل، لكن الاستحالة حينئذ لاستحالة التالي، كما يدل عليه قوله : إذ لو أمكن إلخ . قوله : ( وامتنع حينئذ وجود الأخرى امتناعاً بالغير يدل عليه التقييد بقوله، حينئذ. قوله : ( إذ لو أمكن إلخ) تعليل لقوله، وامتنع إلخ لا لقوله : إذا لم يمكن تعاقبهما على ما وهم . قوله : ( وجد بإيجاد الثانية بذلك الوجود، ليكون التوارد على معلول شخصي . قوله : (لزم إعادة المعدوم الكلام في التوارد لا في الإعادة فلا يرد ما وهم من أن هذا إنما يتم إذا لم يجوز إعادة المعدوم، وإنما لزم الإعادة لأنه لا يجوز أن يكون وجود الثانية في آن عدم الأولى؛ لأنه يلزم وجود المعلول وعدمه معا، إذ المفروض انعدامه في آن عدم الأولى، فيكون وجود الثانية في الآن الثاني، فيكون إعادة للمعدوم . وبهذا اندفع ما قيل : إنه يجوز أن يوجد العلة الثانية في آن عدم العلة الأولى فيزول في ذلك الآن الوجود الحاصل للمعلول بإيجاد الأولى، ويحصل الوجود الآخر بإيجاد الثانية، فلا يلزم إيجاد المعدوم؛ لأن الماهية المعلولة لم تخل عن وجود قط، ولا تحصيل الحاصل إذ الوجود الثاني مغاير للوجود الأول . نعم يلزم توارد الوجودين على طريق تعاقب التصور، ولا بد لإبطاله من دليل آخر يثبت أن المعلول الشخصي إذا زال عنه وجود فعند حصول وجود آخر يزول شخصه، ويصير شخصاً آخر، فلا تتوارد العلتان على معلول واحد بالشخص. قوله: (وجب أن تكون الثانية مفيدة للمعلول أصل وجوده لا أمراً زائداً على وجوده اعتبارياً أو حقيقياً، ليكون علة مستقلة في إفادة ما أفاده الأولى . قوله : ( فإن عدم المعلول بعدم (الأولى أورد عليه أنه يجوز أن يوجد العلة الثانية في آن العلة الأولى، فيزول في ذلك الآن الوجود الحاصل للمعلول بإيجاد الأولى، ويحصل الوجود الآخر بإيجاد الثانية، فلا يلزم إيجاد المعدوم، لأن ماهية المعلول لم تخل عن وجود قط، ولا تحصيل الحاصل، إذ الوجود الثاني مغاير للوجود الأول، نعم يلزم توارد الوجودين على طريق تعاقب الصور، ولا بد لإبطاله من دليل إذ ثبت أن المعلول الشخصي إذا زال عنه وجود فعند حصول وجود آخر تزول شخصيته ويصير شخصاً آخر فلا تتوارد العلتان على معلول واحد
As for their alternation (tawārud) on the basis of substitution (badal) together with the impossibility of conjunction, if their succession (taʿāqub) is not possible, then there is no impossibility in it, by each one of them being such that if it existed initially that individual effect would exist; so when one of them exists, the effect exists, and the existence of the other is then prevented; for if it were possible for the first to cease and the other to exist, then since the effect ceases by the cessation of the first and exists by the bringing into existence of the second, the re-creation of the non-existent (iʿādat al-maʿdūm) would follow. And if it does not cease, then the second must be beneficial to the effect for the original existence it has obtained from the first, so the attainment of the attained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) follows. And it is not possible that the existence of the effect comes from each one of them even with what is in them. And the interpretation of the complete cause (al-ʿilla al-tāmma) as the sum of what the thing depends on is based on what they have established regarding the impermissibility of its multiplicity. His saying: 'If it is not possible, etc.' is understood from it that if their succession is possible, their alternation on the basis of substitution is impossible; but the impossibility then is due to the impossibility of the consequent, as his saying: 'For if it were possible, etc.' indicates. His saying: 'And the existence of the other is then prevented' prevention by another is indicated by the qualification in his saying 'then'. His saying: 'For if it were possible, etc.' is a reason for his saying 'and prevented, etc.' not for his saying 'if their succession is not possible' as was mistakenly thought. His saying: 'Exists by the bringing into existence of the second' with that existence, so that the alternation is upon an individual effect. His saying: 'The re-creation of the non-existent would follow' the discussion is about alternation, not re-creation; so what was mistakenly thought that this is only valid if one does not permit the re-creation of the non-existent is not objected; rather, re-creation necessarily follows because it is not permissible for the existence of the second to be in the moment of the non-existence of the first; for that would entail the existence and non-existence of the effect together, assuming its non-existence in the moment of the non-existence of the first; thus the existence of the second would be in the second moment, and that would be a re-creation of the non-existent. And by this is repelled what was said: it is permissible for the second cause to exist in the moment of the non-existence of the first cause, so that in that moment the existence obtained by the first ceases, and the other existence is obtained by the second; thus no re-creation of the non-existent follows because the essence of the effect never lacked existence, nor attainment of the attained because the second existence is different from the first. Yes, there would be alternation of the two existences on the way of succession of forms, and to refute that another proof is needed that establishes that the individual effect, if existence leaves it, when another existence comes, its individuality disappears and it becomes another individual, so the two causes do not alternate upon one individual effect. His saying: 'It is necessary that the second be beneficial to the effect for its original existence' not for an additional thing over its existence, whether mental or real, so as to be an independent cause in giving what the first gave. His saying: 'For the effect ceases by the cessation of the first' - it was objected that it is permissible for the second cause to exist in the moment of the first cause, so that in that moment the existence obtained by the first ceases, and the other existence is obtained by the second; thus no re-creation of the non-existent follows, because the essence of the effect never lacked existence, nor attainment of the attained, since the second existence is different from the first. Yes, there would be alternation of the two existences on the way of succession of forms; and to refute that another proof is needed since it is established that the individual effect, if existence leaves it, when another existence comes its individuality disappears and it becomes another individual, so the two causes do not alternate upon one individual effect.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a proof that even if two causes act alternately (rather than simultaneously) on a single effect, it remains impossible. The author sets up the scenario: each cause alone would produce the effect if it existed first. When one cause exists, the effect comes to be, and the other cause's existence is prevented. If we imagine the first cause ceasing and the second producing the effect, two problems arise: either the effect ceases with the first cause and then the second re-creates it (which is re-creation of the non-existent, iʿādat al-maʿdūm), or the effect does not cease and the second cause adds nothing new (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). The commentary responds to objections: one might think re-creation could be avoided if the second cause exists in the very moment the first ceases, but that would imply simultaneous existence and non-existence of the effect. Another objection suggests that the second cause could give a different existence, so the effect never lacks existence; the author acknowledges this would involve a succession of two different existences, but then the effect's identity changes, so it is not the same individual. Hence, the two causes do not act upon a single individual effect. The passage concludes that alternation is impossible unless one shows that the effect's individuality survives existence change, which is not the case.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two logical impossibilities arise from alternate causation?
A) Infinite regress and circularity
B) Re-creation of the non-existent and attainment of the attained
C) Simultaneity and succession
D) Causation and contingency

Key Takeaway
Alternate causation collapses into logical impossibility through either re-creation of the non-existent or duplication of the attained.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Causality and Created Things

Resolving Objections: The Individuality of the Effect
Addresses objections and concludes on identity change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes objections to the argument against alternate causation, and why the effect loses its identity when existence changes.

Core Idea
A change in existence changes the identity of the effect.

The previous lesson established two impossible outcomes. However, some might object that the second cause could give a different existence without re-creation or duplication. The author replies by showing that such a change would destroy the effect's individuality, so the two causes would not be acting on the same effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims that the second cause could exist in the same moment the first ceases, so the effect never lacks existence and no re-creation occurs.
This objection tries to avoid 'iʿādat al-maʿdūm'.
2
Understand the reply
The author points out that the second cause would then give a new existence, but the effect's identity depends on its existence; a new existence means a new effect.
This is a key insight: the effect's individuality is tied to its existence.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since the effect's identity changes, the two causes do not act on the same individual effect. Therefore, alternate causation is impossible.
The argument is complete.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the second cause can give a new existence to the same effect without changing its identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something in your life that you think has remained the same despite outward changes—your character, a relationship, a belief. Based on this lesson, if its existence (the way it actually is) has changed, can it truly be the same? How does that challenge you to examine what you think is stable?
?
If your identity is tied to your existence at each moment, then every moment your actions define who you are. What does this mean for how you approach your next decision—especially one you tend to excuse as 'not really me'? Will you own that action as part of your identity?
?
The objection in this lesson tries to preserve the effect's identity while changing its existence. Are there areas in your life where you cling to an identity that no longer matches your actual existence? What steps will you take to align your self-concept with reality?
