علة مستقلة
اجتماع المثلين
الأولوية
الأشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazili argument successfully proves that two independent causes can produce one effect.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that the movement is from God (the true cause) or that the hands together form a single cause, so the independence of each hand is conditional on being alone.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazilites argue that the atom's movement must be from both hands?
A) Because two movements cannot coexist in one atom
B) Because there is no preference for one hand over the other
C) Because the hands are equal in strength
D) Because God creates the movement

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili example of two hands pulling one atom fails because divine causation is the true source of all movement, or because the hands together form a single compound cause.

Rule for Two Similars: Independent Causes for Individuals
Defines the permissibility of independent causes for two similars of the same species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that two similar individuals of the same species can each be caused by independent causes, and that this does not imply the specific nature itself is caused by multiple causes.

Core Idea
Two similar things of the same kind can each have its own independent cause.

This lesson examines a key rule in causality: while a single thing cannot have two independent causes, two things that are identical in species can each be explained by a separate cause. The author clarifies a common misunderstanding and illustrates it with an example of relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's precise wording: 'يجوز تعليله بمستقلتين' and the clarification that follows.
The passage is compact; read slowly to grasp the distinction.
2
Identify the rule and its limitation
Focus on the phrase 'على معنى أن فرداً منه يكون معللاً بعلة مستقلة' and the negation 'لا على معنى أن الطبيعة النوعية توجد...'. Understand that the rule applies to individuals, not the specific nature as a universal.
The common mistake is to extend the permissibility to the specific nature itself.
3
Analyze the example
Look at the example of the relation (المخالفة) between blackness and sweetness. Notice that the two relations are similar in nature but reside in different subjects, allowing each to have an independent cause.
This illustrates the rule concretely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المثلان، فهما واحد بالنوع فيجوز تعليله أي تعليل الواحد بالنوع بمستقلتين، على معنى أن فرداً منه يكون معللاً بعلة مستقلة، وفرداً آخر منه مماثلا للأول يكون معللا بعلة أخرى مستقلة أيضاً، لا على معنى أن الطبيعة النوعية توجد في ضمن الأفراد عن علل متعددة إذ ليس في الأعيان إلا الأشخاص كما مرت إليه الإشارة ) كالمخالفة فإن مخالفة السواد للحلاوة، مثل مخالفة الحلاوة للسواد)، فإن هذين المعروضين، وإن كانا متخالفين في الماهية إلا أن عارضيهما متماثلان فيها.
As for the two similars, they are one in species, so it is permissible to explain it, i.e., explaining the one in species by two independent causes, meaning that an individual of it is explained by an independent cause, and another individual from it similar to the first is explained by another independent cause as well, not meaning that the specific nature exists within individuals from multiple causes since there is nothing in existence except individuals as has been indicated to by the reference, like the relation, for the relation of blackness to sweetness is like the relation of sweetness to blackness, for these two subjects, although they are different in essence, their accidents are similar in it.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage clarifies a key distinction: while one thing in person cannot have two independent causes, two things that are similar (مثلان) and belong to the same species can each be caused by independent causes. The author emphasizes that it is not the specific nature (الطبيعة النوعية) itself that is caused by multiple causes, but rather the individual instances. An example is given: the relation (مخالفة) of blackness to sweetness is similar to the relation of sweetness to blackness. These two relations are in different subjects (blackness and sweetness) but are themselves similar in essence. Each relation can have its own independent cause, whether that cause is the subject alone or the subject with something else. The point is that when multiple individuals share an identical property, each individual's property can be explained by a different cause.

الواحد بالنوع
الطبيعة النوعية
المخالفة
علة مستقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the permissibility extends to the specific nature itself
Correction: The text limits it to individuals; the specific nature as a universal is not caused by multiple causes.

Checkpoint

B) That two similar relations in different subjects can each have an independent cause
C) That blackness and sweetness are identical in essence
D) That the specific nature of a relation cannot be caused by multiple causes

Key Takeaway
Two similar individuals of the same species can each be caused by an independent cause, but the specific nature itself is not caused by multiple causes.

Two Independent Causes: Clarifying 'Mustaqillatayn'
Al-Jurjani glosses the term 'mustaqillatayn' as different in type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator glosses 'mustaqillatayn' as different in type and how this affects the causality of individual particulars.

Core Idea
Two independent causes of the same effect must differ in nature.

Al-Jurjani's gloss on 'mustaqillatayn' sharpens the thesis: an individual cannot have two causes unless they are different in kind. This lesson unpacks that clarification and its implication for causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key terms: الواحد بالشخص, علة مستقلة, مستقلتين, تماثل المعلولين.
Pay attention to the gloss: 'ay mukhtalifatayn' (i.e., different in type).
2
Understand the need for the gloss
Why does the commentator clarify that 'independent' means different in type? Without this, one might mistakenly think any two independent causes could cause one effect.
3
Grasp the logical conclusion
The outcome: similarity of two effects does not force similarity of their causes. This is a key point in debates about causality.
Reflect on how this connects to the broader discussion of causality in Islamic theology.
4
Self-check with the quiz
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm your understanding of the gloss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين

The Fifth Observation - Second Purpose: The individual particular cannot be caused by two independent causes. His saying: (mustaqillatayn) i.e., different in type, so the outcome of the issue is that the similarity of the two effects does not necessitate the similarity of their causes.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage defines the problem and clarifies a key term. The author (al-Iji) states that an individual particular cannot be caused by two independent causes. The commentator (al-Jurjani) explains that 'independent' here means different in type, not just any two causes. The conclusion is that the similarity of two effects does not imply their causes must be similar. This sets up the discussion about whether a species can have multiple causes.

Correction: The commentator explicitly says 'ay mukhtalifatayn' meaning different in type, so the two causes must differ in kind.

The Example of Contrariness: Similar Effects from Different Causes
Al-Iji illustrates the thesis with an example of contrariness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of contrariness between blackness and sweetness demonstrates that similar accidental effects can have distinct independent causes, each tied to its own subject.

Core Idea
Similar effects do not require a single cause; different subjects can produce the same accident independently.

Al-Iji uses the example of contrariness to illustrate a critical nuance in causality: the similarity of an accident across individuals does not imply a shared cause. In this passage, he explains that the contrariness of blackness to sweetness and the contrariness of sweetness to blackness, though the same type of accident, arise from different subjects. This reinforces the idea that in external reality, only individuals exist, and each individual has its own cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the parentheses and try to understand the flow.
The passage contains a parenthetical remark about specific natures.
2
Identify the example and its components
Spot the example: blackness's contrariness to sweetness and sweetness's contrariness to blackness. Note the two subjects (blackness and sweetness) and their accident (contrariness).
Subjects differ in quiddity, accidents are similar.
3
Connect the example to the thesis
Understand that each contrariness is caused by its subject, either alone or with something else. Thus each has an independent cause, supporting the claim that similar effects can have different causes.
This counters the idea of a shared specific nature causing multiple individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is caused by an independent cause, and another individual from it similar to the first is caused by another independent cause as well, not in the sense that the specific nature exists within individuals from multiple causes, since in external reality there are only individuals, as was alluded to earlier. Commentary
This passage presents the example of contrariness to illustrate the thesis. The author states that an individual can have one independent cause, and another similar individual can have a different independent cause. He clarifies that this is not because the specific nature exists in individuals from multiple causes, as only individuals exist. The two subjects differ in quiddity, but the accident of contrariness is similar in quiddity. Each contrariness is caused by its subject, making each have an independent cause. This shows similar effects can have different causes.

Condition for Validity of the Example
The commentator explains the condition for the example's validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific grammatical and ontological condition that must be satisfied for the example of 'the one by species' to be a valid proof in arguments about causality.

Core Idea
The example's validity hinges on a specific grammatical interpretation of the sentence structure.

In dense theological discussions, the validity of an example often depends on precise linguistic and metaphysical assumptions. The commentator here clarifies that the example of 'the one by species' only works if the sentence is understood in a particular way—namely, that it predicates something about the pronoun referent. Understanding this condition is crucial to avoid misapplying the example in causal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic text to get a sense of its grammatical complexity. Notice the conditional phrasing 'إنما يصح عند من يقول...' which sets up the condition.
The passage is dense; a slow reading helps.
2
Identify the condition stated
The condition is: the sentence must be a predicate about the pronoun referent of the subject. Look for this in the translation.
3
Understand the alternative interpretation
The commentator explains why interpreting the example as referring to the two similars directly makes the detailed explanation superfluous.
This shows the necessity of the condition.
4
Reflect on the metaphysical assumption
The passage links the condition to the distinction between the nature considered through individuals versus the nature in essence. This is key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن هذا المثال إنما يصح عند من يقول بأن الجملة الواقعة خبراً عن العائد إلى المبتدأ، وأن يكون ذكر المثلين مستدركاً، إذ يكفي أن يقال : وأما الواحد بالنوع فيجوز تعليله إلخ . وأيضاً الواحد بالنوع هو الأفراد المتفقة الحقيقة، والطبيعة واحد نوعي كما صرح به في بيان أقسام الوحدة، وحمله على أن مقصوده بيان وجه أفراد الضمير مع كونه راجعاً إلى المثلين وهو تأويلهما بالواحد بالنوع يأبى عنه قوله على معنى أن فرداً منه إلخ، فإنه صريح في أن المعلل هو الطبيعة باعتبار الأفراد لا بحسب الذات، ولأن ذلك التفصيل إنما يحتاج إليه إذا كان المعلل هو الطبيعة النوعية، وأما إذا كان المعلل المثلان، فلا حاجة إلى ذلك، بل يصير مستدركاً.
But this example is only valid according to one who says that the sentence occurring as a predicate about the pronoun referent of the subject, and that mentioning the two similars is superfluous, for it suffices to say: 'As for the one by species, it is permissible to cause it' etc. Also, the one by species is individuals agreeing in reality, and the nature is one by species, as was stated in the explanation of the divisions of unity. And interpreting it as that his intention is to explain the aspect of the pronoun's referents while it refers to the two similars—which is interpreting them as the one by species—is precluded by his statement 'on the meaning that an individual from it' etc., for it is explicit that the caused is the nature in consideration of individuals, not in respect of essence. And because that detailed explanation is only needed if the caused is the specific nature; but if the caused is the two similars, there is no need for that, rather it becomes superfluous.

Commentary
This passage explains a condition for the example to be valid: it depends on a grammatical interpretation regarding the sentence structure. The commentator argues that the correct understanding is that the caused thing is the nature considered through individuals, not the nature itself. If the caused were the two similars directly, the detailed explanation would be unnecessary and even superfluous. This clarifies that the example supports the thesis only when seen as the nature existing in individuals.

The Proper Wording of the Thesis
Al-Jurjani defends the author's choice of absolute wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author uses the absolute phrase 'by two independent causes' without specifying difference, and how the argument of the negators applies to both types.

Core Idea
The negationist argument applies to all independent causes, so the thesis wording is right.

In this lesson, we see al-Jurjani defend the author's choice of wording against a criticism that he should have specified 'different in type'. The commentator explains that the argument of those who deny the position actually covers both agreeing and differing causes, making the absolute phrase sufficient. Understanding this clarifies the scope of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two views
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the criticism (it would be more appropriate to say 'different in type') and the truth (the negators' proof negates both).
Pay attention to the phrase 'by primary inference' (بالطريق الأولى).
2
Focus on the argument from primary inference
The commentator says the examples with different types imply the permissibility of agreeing types by primary inference (a fortiori). Understand why this strengthens the author's absolute wording.
3
Reflect on why the absolute wording is justified
Consider that if the negators' proof applies to both differing and agreeing causes, then there is no need to specify difference. The absolute term covers everything.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أي تعليل الواحد بالنوع بمستقلتين قيل : كان الأنسب أن يقول: بمستقلتين مختلفتين بالنوع إذ هو المتنازع فيه، وأما التعليل بمستقلتين متفقتين بالنوع، فلا نزاع لأحد في جوازه. والحق أن دليل النافين ينفي جواز تعليل الواحد بالنوع بمستقلتين مطلقاً سواء كانتا مختلفتين بالنوع أو متفقتين وهو الذي أشار إليه المصنف بقوله : فإن قيل إلخ، فلذا اكتفى المصنف في عنوان البحث بمستقلتين مطلقا، وإنما أوردوا في مقام الاستدلال تعليله بمختلفتين لدلالته على جواز تعليله بمتفقتين بالطريق الأولى.
His saying: i.e., causing the one by species by two independent causes. It was said: It would be more appropriate to say: by two independent causes different in type, for that is the point of dispute. As for causing by two independent causes agreeing in type, no one disputes its permissibility. But the truth is that the proof of the negators negates the permissibility of causing the one by species by two independent causes absolutely, whether they are different in type or agreeing in type, and that is what the author referred to by his saying 'If it is said' etc. Therefore, the author sufficed with 'by two independent causes' absolutely in the title of the discussion. They only adduced in the context of proof causing it by two different ones because it implies the permissibility of causing it by two agreeing ones by primary inference.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential criticism about the wording of the thesis. Some might say the author should have said 'by two independent causes different in type' since that is the point of dispute. The commentator explains that the negationist argument actually denies both types—whether the causes are different or agreeing in type. Therefore, the author's absolute wording is sufficient. The examples given later with different types are meant to imply the permissibility of agreeing types by stronger reason (a fortiori).

Correction: The negators' proof negates both differing and agreeing types, so the dispute covers all types of independent causes.

Checkpoint

The Example of Heat from Different Causes
Al-Iji gives an example of heat from different sources and clarifies conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji uses the example of heat from different sources to illustrate the causation of similar effects by different causes, including the condition of quiddity similarity and the distinction between existence in itself and relational attribution.

Core Idea
Similar effects can have different causes, but only if they are truly identical in essence.

This lesson examines a key example from al-Iji that tests our understanding of causality. He uses the case of heat produced by fire, the sun, and motion to argue that similar effects can stem from different independent causes. However, he immediately qualifies this with an important condition about the true similarity of the effects and distinguishes between an accident's inherent existence and its attribution to a subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument and the key terms.
Notice the parentheses and brackets from OCR; they indicate editorial additions.
2
Identify the condition for validity
The author states that the heat example is only valid if the individuals of heat are completely similar in quiddity. Why is this condition necessary?
Consider what would happen if heat individuals were not exactly similar.
3
Understand the distinction of existence
The passage contrasts existence in itself (al-wujud al-nafsi) with relational existence (al-wujud al-ribati). How does the subject affect attribution but not the accident's own existence?
This distinction resolves potential objections about multiple causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فالحرارة نوع واحد، ثم يعلل فرد منها بالنار، وفرد بالشمس وفرد بالحركة)، فقد عللت المتماثلات بعلل مختلفة مستقلة هي هذه الأمور وحدها، أو مأخوذة مع غيرها، لكن هذا المثال إنما يصح إذا كانت أفراد الحرارة متماثلة متفقة في تمام الماهية (وسننبه على عدم تماثل أفرادها فيما بعد ) ، وإنما لم يمثلوا بأفراد الحرارة النارية المستندة إلى أفراد النار ؛ لعدم تعدد العلل هاهنا فإن العلة طبيعة النار، كما أن المعلول طبيعة الحرارة، وإن اعتبر أفرادهما كان كل من العلة والمعلول متعدداً . قال في نفسه لا باعتبار وجودهما الرابطي، أعني اتصاف المحل بهما كما نبه عليه بقوله : إذ ليس في الأعيان إلا الأشخاص، كيف وتعليلهما من حيث الاتصاف بعلتين مختلفتين، مما لا شبهة فيه، إذ للمحل مدخل في الاتصاف، وهو قد يكون مختلفاً فيهما بخلاف وجودهما في نفسه، فإنه لا مدخل للمحل فيه، بل تشخصهما .
And also, heat is one type, then one individual of it is caused by fire, one by the sun, and one by motion. So similar things have been caused by different independent causes, these matters alone or taken with others. But this example is only valid if the individuals of heat are similar, agreeing in complete quiddity (and we will point out later that their individuals are not similar). And they only did not give the example of individuals of fire-heat due to the individuals of fire because there is no multiplicity of causes here, for the cause is the nature of fire, just as the effect is the nature of heat. And if their individuals are considered, both the cause and the effect become multiple. He said: in itself, not in terms of its relational existence, i.e., the attribution of the subject to them, as he pointed out by his saying: 'For in external reality there are only individuals.' How so? Their causation in terms of attribution by two different causes is not questionable, for the subject has an entry in attribution, and it may differ in them unlike their existence in themselves, for there is no entry of the subject in it, but rather their individuation.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents another example: heat from fire, sun, and motion. It argues that similar effects (heat individuals) have different independent causes. However, it notes that this example is only valid if the heat individuals are completely similar in quiddity, which the author says will later be disputed. The reason they did not use fire-heat from fire individuals is that there is no multiplicity of causes—the cause is the nature of fire and effect the nature of heat. The commentary further distinguishes between the existence of an accident in itself and its attribution to a subject; the subject only affects attribution, not the accident's own existence.

Key Takeaway
The validity of attributing similar effects to different causes depends on the effects being identical in quiddity, and causation must consider the subject's role in attribution, not just the accident's own essence.

?

?

The Genus Example Refuted and Unity of Causes
Al-Jurjani refutes the genus example from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the example of the genus being caused by different species is invalid, and why a single species can be caused by multiple causes.

Core Idea
Genus and species are not distinct in external existence; the genus is a concomitant of the species.

In this passage, al-Jurjani critiques a common example about causality: that the nature of a genus is caused by its different species (differentiae). He shows that this example only works if genus and species are separate in external reality, which is false. He then clarifies the correct view that a single species (the 'one by species') can indeed have multiple causes, using the relation between genus and species as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the faulty assumption
The example assumes genus and species are distinct in external existence. Al-Jurjani says this is false.
The example comes from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya.
2
Grasp the correct relation between genus and species
Genus natures are external concomitants of species; the genus is constituted by the species.
This shows the genus is not a separate cause.
3
Apply to the 'one by species' issue
The correct view is that a single type (one by species) can be caused by multiple causes, as with genera deriving from species.
The dispute is about causation of the real type, not a general type.
4
Distinguish real type from general type
The heat example is critiqued; the commentator clarifies that the real type (ḥaqīqī) is what matters.
Multiplicity in persons does not harm the specific unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التمثيل بأن طبيعة الجنس معللة بفصول مختلفة، فإنما يصح على تقدير تمايز الجنس والفصل في الوجود الخارجي، وقد عرفت بطلانه. قوله : ( وأما التمثيل بأن طبيعة إلخ) رد لما في المباحث المشرقية، وأما الواحد النوعي، فالصحيح جواز استناده إلى علل كثيرة وكيف لا أقول بذلك وطبائع الأجناس لوازم خارجية للفصول، وهي معلولاتها فإن الجنس إنما يتقوم في الوجود بسبب اقتران الفصل به. قوله : (فإن العلة إلخ) يعني سواء نظر إلى الطبيعتين أو إلى الأفراد، والمتحقق هاهنا تعليل واحد بواحد لا تعليل واحد بمتعدد. قوله : (كان) كل من العلة والمعلول متعدداً أي كان كل منهما متعدداً بالشخص، مع الحرارة من نوع واحد تدفع بأن المراد بالنوع ما هو أعم من الحقيقي وأنت خبير بأن المتنازع فيه تعليل الواحد بالنوع الحقيقي بمختلفتين، وأن قوله : أيضاً فالحرارة إلخ في حكم الاستدلال على جواز ذلك التعليل، فلذا لم يلتفت الشارح إلى ما ذكره . قوله : ( وإنما لم يمثلوا بأفراد الحرارة النارية) تعريض بشارح المقاصد حيث مثل به . قوله : ( وإن اعتبر أفرادهما كان كل من العلة والمعلول متعدداً قيل : المراد من قوله : كان كل من العلة والمعلول متعدداً أن الكلام كان في وحدة المعلول مع تعدد العلل، والتعدد على هذا التوجيه في كل من العلة والمعلول. ونقل كلام الملخص ليرتبط به قوله : فإن قيل إلخ، لأن هذا السؤال والجواب من كلام الإمام، وفيه : أن هذا وإن كان متبادراً إلى الفهم من مساق الكلام، حيث تعرض لتعدد المعلول أيضاً، إلا أن تعدد اللازم مما ذكر تعدد شخص، فلا يضر بالوحدة النوعية التي كلامنا فيها. فالوجه أن يقال : المراد مما ذكره أن المستفاد منه مجرد التعدد من
As for the example that the nature of the genus is caused by different species (differentiae), it is only valid on the assumption that the genus and species are distinct in external existence, and you know its falsity. His saying: (As for the example that the nature etc.) is a refutation of what is in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya. And as for the one by species, the correct view is the permissibility of its being based on many causes. How could I not say that, while the natures of genera are external concomitants of species, and they are effects of them? For the genus is only constituted in existence through the conjunction of the species with it. His saying: (For the cause etc.) means whether one looks at the two natures or at the individuals, what is realized here is a causation of one by one, not of one by many. His saying: (It was) each of the cause and effect is multiple means each of them is multiple in person, with the heat being of one type. It is refuted by saying that what is meant by 'type' is more general than the real type. And you are aware that the point of dispute is the causation of the one by the real type by two different ones, and that his saying 'also heat' etc. is in the position of evidence for the permissibility of that causation, so the commentator did not pay attention to what he mentioned. His saying: (And they only did not give the example of individuals of fire-heat) is an implicit criticism of the author of Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid where he gave that example. His saying: (And if their individuals are considered, each of the cause and effect becomes multiple). And he transmitted the words of al-Mulakhkhaṣ to connect with his saying 'If it is said' etc., because this question and answer are from the words of the Imam. In it: that this, although it is immediately understood from the context of the discourse, since it also deals with the multiplicity of the effect, but the multiplicity of the necessary (lāzim) from what was mentioned is a multiplicity of person, so it does not harm the specific unity that we are discussing. So the correct view is to say: The meaning of what he mentioned is that what is learned from it is merely multiplicity...

Commentary
This passage refutes the example that the nature of a genus is caused by different species (differentiae). The commentator argues that genus and species are not distinct in external reality; the genus is a concomitant of the species and is constituted by it. The correct view is that the one by species can have many causes. The commentary also addresses objections about the heat example, clarifying that the dispute is about the real type (ḥaqīqī) and that the heat example serves as evidence for the permissibility of multiple causes. The final part deals with the interpretation of 'each of cause and effect becomes multiple,' explaining that the multiplicity is of persons and does not affect the specific unity discussed.

A Single Individual Cannot Be Caused by Two Independent Causes
Sanusi presents the principle and refutes examples of opposition and heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single individual cannot have two independent causes, and how apparent counterexamples like heat from different sources are resolved in kalam.

Core Idea
A single individual cannot have two independent causes—no exception.

Sanusi tackles a foundational principle of causality: no single individual can be produced by two independent causes. He examines key examples—opposition, genus and differentia, and heat—to show that they do not actually violate this principle. This lesson clarifies the subtle reasoning behind each case.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Notice the flow from the principle to the examples and their refutations.
Pay attention to the specific terms: الواحد بالشخص, العلة المستقلة.
2
Identify the principle and the three examples
The principle is stated first: a single individual cannot have two independent causes. Then three examples are brought: opposition (relational), genus-differentia, and heat from different sources. Mark where each begins.
3
Understand why the genus example fails
Sanusi says the genus-differentia example only works if genus and differentia are distinct in external existence, which is false. This is a key point about the unity of essence.
Recall from earlier lessons that genus and differentia are mental distinctions, not separate in reality.
4
Grasp the resolution of the heat example
The heat example seems to contradict the principle, but Sanusi explains that it only holds if the individuals of heat are identical in complete essence—which they are not, as he will later show. Thus the example does not refute the principle.
Also note why they didn't use individuals of heat from different fires: because the cause is the nature of fire, not individual fires.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين المخالفة التي هي من الإضافات أمر ثبوتي موجود في الخارج، وكذا الحال في التمثيل بالمضادة بين السواد والبياض. وأما التمثيل بأن طبيعة الجنس معللة بفصول مختلفة، فإنما يصح على تقدير تمايز الجنس والفصل في الوجود الخارجي، وقد عرفت بطلانه. وأيضاً فالحرارة نوع واحد، ثم يعلل فرد منها بالنار، وفرد بالشمس وفرد بالحركة، فقد عللت المتماثلات بعلل مختلفة مستقلة هي هذه الأمور وحدها، أو مأخوذة مع غيرها، لكن هذا المثال إنما يصح إذا كانت أفراد الحرارة متماثلة متفقة في تمام الماهية وسننبه على عدم تماثل أفرادها فيما بعد، وإنما لم يمثلوا بأفراد الحرارة النارية المستندة إلى أفراد النار؛ لعدم تعدد العلل هاهنا فإن العلة طبيعة النار، كما أن المعلول طبيعة الحرارة، وإن اعتبر أفرادهما كان كل من العلة والمعلول متعدداً.
The Fifth Observation - Second Aim: The one in person is not caused by two independent causes. Opposition, which is among relations, is a positive attribute existing externally. The same applies to the example of contrariety between black and white. As for the example that the nature of the genus is caused by different differentiae, it is only valid on the assumption that the genus and differentia are distinct in external existence, and you have already known its falsity. Also, heat is one type; then one individual of it is caused by fire, another by the sun, and another by motion. So similar things have been caused by different independent causes that are these things alone or taken with others. But this example is only valid if the individuals of heat are similar and identical in the complete essence. We will later draw attention to the non-similarity of its individuals. They did not give examples from individuals of heat caused by individuals of fire because there is no multiplicity of causes here; for the cause is the nature of fire, just as the effect is the nature of heat. If their individuals are considered, both the cause and the effect become multiple.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the principle that an individual entity (one in person) cannot have two independent causes. The author begins by giving an example: opposition, which is a relational attribute, is a positive existing thing; similarly, the relation of contrariety between black and white is one relation, not two. Then he refutes the example of the genus being caused by multiple differentiae, because genus and differentia are not distinct in external existence. The main refutation is the heat example: heat is one type, but different individuals are caused by fire, sun, or motion. This seems to violate the principle. However, the author explains that this example only works if the individuals of heat are identical in every aspect of their essence. He notes that he will later show they are not identical. He also explains why they did not give the example of heat from different fires: in that case the cause is the nature of fire as a whole, and the effect is the nature of heat as a whole; only when considering individuals does multiplicity occur. Thus the principle stands.

Key Terms
الواحد بالشخص
العِلَّةُ المُسْتَقِلَّة
المخالفة
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of heat from different sources (fire, sun, motion) disproves the principle that a single individual cannot have two independent causes.
Correction: The individuals of heat caused by different sources are not identical in complete essence, so they are not the same individual. Therefore the principle is not violated.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is the example of heat from fire, sun, and motion not a valid counterexample to the principle that one individual cannot have two independent causes?
A) Because the causes are not truly independent.
B) Because the individuals of heat are not identical in essence.
C) Because heat is not a real type.
D) Because fire, sun, and motion are all the same cause.

Key Takeaway
The principle that one individual cannot have two independent causes remains intact when we properly analyze examples like heat, as the individuals involved are not identical in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing, do you ever mentally divide its cause between your own effort and Allah's provision as if each were independent? How does Sanusi's principle—that a single individual cannot have two independent causes—call you to unify your attribution of all effects to Allah alone?
?
The heat example shows that things appearing identical (like two cups of hot water) may differ in essence. Have you ever considered that your outwardly similar acts of worship (e.g., two prayers) might differ in sincerity? What would it mean for you to ensure that each act of worship has a single, pure intention solely for Allah?

Objection and Answer on Type-Nature Causation
Objection that a type-nature cannot be caused by two different causes and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection argues that a single type-nature cannot be caused by two different causes and how the answer resolves this by distinguishing between the need for a cause in general versus a specific cause.

Core Idea
A type-nature needs a cause in general, not a specific one — specification comes from the cause.

This passage presents a classic kalam objection and its answer regarding the causal relationship between a universal nature and multiple particular causes. The objection seems to force a dilemma: either the nature requires a specific cause, which would exclude others, or it does not, in which case it is independent. The answer clarifies that the nature's need for a cause is general, not specific, allowing multiple specific causes to bring about individuals of the same type. Let us examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the objection
Locate the condition expressed by 'فإن قيل' (for if it is said) and understand the dilemma presented: either the nature requires a specific cause, leading to exclusivity, or it does not, leading to independence.
The objection uses the phrase 'إلا استغنت عنهما' (otherwise it would be independent of both).
3
Analyze the answer's distinction
In the answer (قلنا), notice the key distinction: the nature necessitates the need for some cause (علة ما), not a specific one. The specification of which cause is from the side of the cause itself.
The phrase 'والتعيين من جانب العلة' (and the specification is from the side of the cause) is crucial.
4
Understand the conclusion
The answer concludes that it is possible for similar individuals of the same type to be caused by different specific causes. This clarifies that the debate is about individuals, not the universal itself.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves the possibility of multiple causal pathways for the same type of effect, which is important in discussions of divine action and natural causality.
This connects to broader kalam debates about the relationship between God and secondary causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الملخص : المعلول الواحد بالنوع يجوز استناده إلى علل مختلفة بالنوع، فإن قيل: الماهية النوعية إن اقتضت لذاتها أو للوازمها الحاجة إلى إحداهما علل الأمران أي الفردان المتماثلان منها بها، أي بتلك الإحدى بعينها؛ لأن مقتضى ذات الشيء أو لازمه يستحيل انفكاكه عنه، وإلا استغنت عنهما أي عن كل واحدة من العلتين فلا تعلل تلك الماهية النوعية بشيء منهما؛ لامتناع تعليل الشيء بما هو مستغن عنه. قلنا: هي أي تلك الماهية تقتضي الاحتياج إلى علة ما والتعيين من جانب العلة أي نختار أن الماهية لا تحتاج إلى شيء بعينه من العلتين المفروضتين، بل هي محتاجة إلى علة ما لا بعينها، ولا يلزم من ذلك أن لا تكون الماهية معللة بالعلتين المعينتين، لجواز أن استناد اتحاد أفراد كل منهما في الحقيقة وليس المقصود هاهنا جواز تعليل الأفراد المتماثلة من المعلول الواحد بالنوع بالأفراد المتماثلة من العلة الواحدة بالنوع، بل جواز تعليل الأفراد المتماثلة بعلل مختلفة.
The Summary: The one effect in type may be attributed to different causes in type. For if it is said: the type-nature, if it necessitates by itself or by its concomitants the need for one of them, then the two things, i.e. the two similar individuals of it, are caused by that specific one; because the requirement of a thing's essence or its concomitant is impossible to separate from it. Otherwise, it would be independent of both, i.e. of each one of the two causes, so that type-nature would not be caused by anything from them; because it is impossible to cause a thing by that which it is independent of. We say: it, i.e. that nature, necessitates the need for some cause, and the specification is from the side of the cause. That is, we choose that the nature does not need a specific thing from the two assumed causes, but it needs some unspecified cause. And it does not follow from that that the nature is not caused by the two specific causes, because it is possible that the attribution of the union of individuals of each of them is in reality, and the purpose here is not the permissibility of causing similar individuals of one type-effect by similar individuals of one type-cause, but the permissibility of causing similar individuals by different causes.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding whether a single type-nature can be caused by two different types of causes. The objection, introduced by 'فإن قيل', argues as follows: If the type-nature requires a specific cause by its essence or by a concomitant, then the two similar individuals of that nature would be caused by that specific cause alone and not the other. If the nature does not require that specific cause, then it is independent of it, so it cannot be caused by it at all. In either case, the nature cannot be caused by two different causes. The answer, introduced by 'قلنا', responds: The nature requires some cause in general, but the specification of which cause is from the side of the cause, not the nature. The nature does not specify a particular cause; it only needs a cause. Therefore, it is possible for the nature to be caused by two specific causes, each causing different individuals, as long as the nature itself does not determine which cause. The answer clarifies that the discussion is about causing similar individuals (of the same type) by different causes, not about causing the type-nature itself by multiple causes.

Key Terms
المعلول الواحد بالنوع
الماهية النوعية
الاحتياج
التعيين
علة ما

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A type-nature must require a specific cause if it is to be caused.
Correction: The answer shows that the nature only necessitates the need for some cause in general, not a specific one. The specification comes from the cause itself, so multiple specific causes are possible.

Checkpoint
In the response to the objection, what does the answer say the type-nature necessitates?
A) A specific cause from among the two.
B) The need for some cause, with specification from the cause.
C) Independence from all causes.
D) That the two causes must be identical.

Key Takeaway
A single type-nature can be caused by different specific causes because its need for a cause is general, not specific.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a situation in your life where you desperately wanted God to act through a specific means (a person, a circumstance). When that means didn't materialize, did you feel God had abandoned you? This lesson teaches that our need is for a cause in general, not a specific one. How can you broaden your trust in Allah to work through any means He chooses, without dictating the how? What practical step will you take this week to surrender your expectations of how Allah should help you?

The objection in this lesson assumes that if a type-nature requires a cause, it must be a specific cause. In your own life, do you sometimes assume that your problems or needs have a single, fixed solution from Allah? When that solution doesn't appear, do you feel stuck or doubt His care? How does the answer—that only a cause in general is needed—free you to see multiple possibilities for divine help? The Singular Individual and Two Independent Causes: Objection and Answer
Jarjani presents the principle, an objection, and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that a single individual cannot be caused by two independent causes, and how the objection based on specific essence is answered by distinguishing between need for a cause and specification from the cause.

Core Idea
A single effect needs a cause in general, not a specific one—specification comes from the cause itself.

In this passage from al-Mawaqif, al-Jurjani presents a key causal principle: a singular individual cannot have two independent causes. He then addresses an objection that seems to challenge this, using the notion of a specific essence. The answer, attributed to Imam al-Razi, resolves the dilemma by separating the essence's need for some cause from the determination of which cause it is. This lesson unpacks the argument and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle and objection
Read the passage once to get the overall flow: the principle, the objection with its two branches, and the answer. Notice the technical terms like 'واحد بالشخص' and 'ماهية نوعية'.
The objection is a classical dilemma: either the essence necessitates a specific cause or it does not.
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objection argues that if the specific essence requires a cause, it must be a specific one; if it does not, it is independent and cannot be caused. This seems to undermine the possibility of two causes for one effect.
3
Analyze the answer's distinction
The answer differentiates between the essence's need for a cause (علة ما) and the specification of which cause (تعيين من جانب العلة). The essence needs a cause in general, but it is the cause that determines itself as the cause for that essence.
Thus, the essence can be caused by both specific causes without being dependent on either's particularity.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how this answer preserves the original principle: the singular individual is not caused by two independent causes in a way that makes it dependent on both, because its need is for a cause generically.
This shows sophisticated causal reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين الملخص : المعلول الواحد بالنوع يجوز استناده إلى علل مختلفة بالنوع، فإن قيل: الماهية النوعية إن اقتضت لذاتها أو للوازمها الحاجة إلى إحداهما علل الأمران أي الفردان المتماثلان منها بها، أي بتلك الإحدى بعينها؛ لأن مقتضى ذات الشيء، أو لازمه يستحيل انفكاكه عنه وإلا وإن لم تقتض الحاجة إلى إحداهما استغنت عنهما أي عن كل واحدة من العلتين فلا تعلل تلك الماهية النوعية بشيء منهما؛ لامتناع تعليل الشيء بما هو مستغن عنه. قلنا: هي أي تلك الماهية تقتضي الاحتياج إلى علة ما والتعيين من جانب العلة أي نختار أن الماهية لا تحتاج إلى شيء بعينه من العلتين المفروضتين، بل هي محتاجة إلى علة ما لا بعينها، ولا يلزم من ذلك أن لا تكون الماهية معللة بالعلتين المعينتين، لجواز أن يكون تعليلها بالمعينة ناشئاً من جانب العلة بأن تكون هذه المعينة تقتضي أن تكون علة لتلك الماهية، وتلك المعينة أيضاً تقتضي أن تكون علة لها، فهي مع استغنائها عن خصوصية كل منهما تكون معللة بهما كذا ذكره الإمام الرازي
The Fifth Observatory - Second Objective: The singular individual is not caused by two independent causes. The epitome: The effect that is one in type may be attributed to different causes in type. For if it is said: The specific essence, if it necessitates by itself or by its concomitants the need for one of the two causes, then the two like individuals are caused by that one specific cause; because what the essence or its concomitant necessitates cannot be separated from it. Otherwise, if it does not necessitate the need for one of them, it dispenses with both, i.e., with each of the two causes, so that specific essence is not caused by any of them, due to the impossibility of causing something by what it is independent of. We say: That essence necessitates the need for some cause, and specification is from the side of the cause. We choose that the essence does not need anything specific from the two supposed causes, but rather it needs some unspecified cause, and it does not follow from that that the essence is not caused by the two specific causes, because it is possible that its being caused by the specific cause arises from the side of the cause, such that this specific cause necessitates that it be a cause for that essence, and that other specific cause also necessitates that it be a cause for it. So it, while being independent of the particularity of each, is caused by both. This is what Imam al-Razi mentioned.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents a central principle in kalam ontology: a single individual (واحد بالشخص) cannot be caused by two independent causes. It then presents an objection based on the idea of a specific essence (الماهية النوعية) and answers it. The passage first quotes Al-Mulakhkhas stating that an effect one in type can be attributed to different types of causes. The objection argues that if the specific essence requires a cause by itself or its concomitants, then it must be caused by one specific cause; if it does not require a cause, it is independent and cannot be caused by any. The answer, attributed to Imam al-Razi, distinguishes between the essence's need for some cause (علة ما) and the specification of which cause, which comes from the side of the cause itself. Thus, the essence is caused by both specific causes without being dependent on either's particularity. This resolves the dilemma.

Key Terms
واحد بالشخص
علتين مستقلتين
ماهية نوعية
اقتضت
علة ما
تعيين من جانب العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that being caused by two causes implies the effect is dependent on the particularity of each cause, making the principle invalid.
Correction: The effect's need is for a cause in general, not for any specific cause. Specification comes from the cause's side, so the effect is not independent but also not dependent on the particularity of each cause.

Checkpoint
According to the answer attributed to Imam al-Razi, what does the specific essence (الماهية النوعية) necessitate?
A) Need for a specific cause
B) Need for some cause, with specification from the cause's side
C) Independence from all causes
D) Need for two specific causes

Key Takeaway
A single effect can have multiple causes as long as its need is for a cause in general, not for a specific one; specification comes from the cause's side.

Do you ever find yourself attributing your successes or failures to specific people or circumstances as if they are the only possible causes, forgetting that your real need is for a cause in general—ultimately Allah? How willing are you to let go of those specific attachments and trust that Allah can provide through any means?

Resolving the Problem of Causal Dependence: The Role of Specific Causes
Al-Jarjani critiques the answer, presents the summary view, and resolves the problem using al-Katibi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jarjani resolves the apparent contradiction of an effect needing two independent causes by distinguishing essential need from accidental need.

Core Idea
An effect needs some cause essentially, but a specific cause accidentally.

In this passage, al-Jarjani tackles a tricky objection: if the effect needs only 'some cause' in general, how can it depend on two specific causes? The answer, he explains, lies in distinguishing between the effect's essential need for a cause in general and the accidental need for a specific cause once it exists. This resolution, drawn from al-Katibi, preserves the causal principle without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Read the opening sentence where al-Jarjani critiques the previous answer for entailing a commitment (التزام). The key point: the effect doesn't truly need the specific cause; it only needs some cause.
2
Examine the summary view
Identify the تلخيص النظر which summarizes the view of Imam al-Razi. Note the distinction: absolute need from the effect's side, specification from the cause's side.
3
Follow the refutation of Sharh al-Maqasid
Al-Jarjani refutes a misunderstanding by the author of Sharh al-Maqasid. Clarify that the original answer only negates essential need for a specific cause, not essential independence.
4
Grasp al-Katibi's resolution
Read the final part where al-Katibi's solution is presented. The effect's need for a specific cause is accidental. If two causes coincide, each would be needed specifically, causing a problem. But if they alternate, no problem.
This resolves the issue by showing that the effect's need for a specific cause is not essential but conditional on the cause's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف : ( واعلم أن هذا الجواب فيه التزام؛ لعدم احتياج المعلول إلى العلة بعينها، مع كونها محتاجة إلى علة ما لا بعينها فإن الماهية إذا كانت معللة بعلة معينة لا لاحتياجها إليها، بل لاقتضاء تلك المعينة أن تكون علة للماهية، فقد جاز عدم احتياج المعلول إلى ما هو علة له حقيقة فلا يلزم احتياج الشخص المعلول للعلتين المستقلتين إلى كل منهما أي إلى شيء منهما بعينه، بل احتياجه إلى مفهوم أحدهما أي إلى علة ما، الذي لا ينافي الاجتماع). وتلخيص النظر: هي هي سره، لكن عبارة الإمام في المباحث صريحة في هذا المعنى حيث قال: فإن المعلول يحتاج إلى علة ما، ثم إن استناده إلى تلك المعينة بعينها ليس لأمر عائد إلى المعلول، بل لأن ذات العلة لما العلة مقتضية لذلك المعلول، فالحاجة المطلقة من جانب المعلول، وتعيين من جانبها. ولعل في قول الشارح قدس سره، كذا ذكره الإمام إشارة خفية إلى ما قلنا. قوله : (تكون معللة بهما والتعليل بهما لا يقتضي الاحتياج إليهما بخصوصهما، ولا يلزم اجتماع الاستغناء والاحتياج. قوله : ( إلى ما هو علة له حقيقة وهي المعينة، فإنها المعطية لوجودها، لا المطلقة. قوله : ( إلى شيء منهما ) أي ليس المراد رفع الإيجاب الكلي، كما هو المتبادر، بل الكلي، وهو ظاهر. قوله : (فهي مع استغنائها إلخ فيه رد على شارح المقاصد حيث قال في تلخيص هذا الجواب الذي نقل عن الإمام والحاصل أن الماهية النوعية بالنظر إلى ذاتها ليست محتاجة إلى العلة المعينة ولا غنية عنها، بل كل من ذلك بالعارض. ووجه الرد أن الذي ذكره الإمام في الجواب نفي احتياج الماهية النوعية بالذات إلى خصوصية كل من العلتين، لا نفي استغنائها بالذات عنها، وهو الظاهر. قوله: (وتلخيص النظر إلخ) الجواب عن هذا النظر مستفاد من كلام الكاتبي في شرح الملخص حيث قال : المعلول بحسب الذات وإن لم يكن مفتقراً إلى هذه العلة المعينة، لكنه مفتقر إلى علة ما وتلك العلة المعينة لما وجدت وأوجدت المعلول عرض للمعلول الافتقار إليها. وتقرير هذا الجواب : هاهنا أن المعلول الشخصي إذا اجتمع عليه علتان مستقلتان تعين كل واحدة منهما، احتياج المعلول إلى نفسها على ما تقدم من أن تعين العلة من جانبها، فيلزم احتياج المعلول إلى كل واحدة منهما بعينها، ويعود المحذور. ولهذا إذا لم يجتمعا، بل تواردا لم يلزم محذور، إذ المتعين للعلية على تقدير وجود كل واحدة منهما إنما هو الموجود حينئذ
The author said: Know that this answer entails a commitment; because the effect does not need the specific cause, while it needs some unspecified cause. For if the essence is caused by a specific cause not due to its need for it, but due to that specific cause's necessitating that it be a cause for the essence, then it is possible that the effect does not need what is truly its cause. So there follows no need of the singular individual effect for each of the two independent causes, i.e., for anything specific from them, but rather its need is for the concept of one of them, i.e., for some cause, which does not preclude coexistence. And the summary of the view: ... It is its secret, but the words of the Imam in the Investigations are explicit in this meaning where he said: The effect needs some cause; then its attribution to that specific cause is not due to something pertaining to the effect, but because the essence of the cause, since it is a cause, necessitates that effect. So the absolute need is from the side of the effect, and specification is from its side. Perhaps in the commentator's words, 'thus mentioned by Imam al-Razi,' there is a subtle indication to what we said. His saying: 'It is caused by both, and causing by both does not necessitate need for each specifically, nor does it entail the coexistence of independence and need.' His saying: 'to what is truly its cause, which is the specific cause, for it gives existence, not the absolute.' His saying: 'to anything from them' means the intent is not to negate the universal affirmative, as understood, but the universal affirmative? It is apparent. His saying: 'So it, while being independent...' etc. contains a refutation of the author of Al-Maqasid where he said in summarizing this answer transmitted from the Imam: 'The essence in itself is neither in need of the specific cause nor independent of it; rather each is accidental.' The point of refutation is that what the Imam mentioned in the answer is the negation of the essence's essential need for the particularity of each cause, not the negation of its essential independence from them, and it is apparent. His saying: 'And the summary of the view...' etc. The answer to this summary is derived from the words of al-Katibi in his commentary on Al-Mulakhkhas, where he said: The effect, in terms of essence, although not in need of this specific cause, is in need of some cause, and that specific cause, when it exists and produces the effect, the effect accidentally becomes in need of it. The exposition of this answer here is that the singular individual effect, when two independent causes coincide upon it, each one becomes specific, and the effect's need is for itself, as previously established that specification of the cause is from its side. Then the effect's need for each specific cause necessarily follows, and the problem returns. Therefore, if they do not coincide but rather alternate, no problem arises, for the one determined for causality upon the existence of each is the one existing then.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage contains the author's critical remark on the previous answer and the resolution of the summary view. The author (al-Jarjani) notes that the answer entails a commitment: the effect does not actually need the specific cause that truly gives it existence, since the specific cause is only effective because it necessarily produces the effect, not because the effect needs it. The summary of the view (تلخيص النظر) is then presented, and it is explained that Imam al-Razi's words in al-Mabahith support this: the effect needs some cause, but its dependence on a specific cause is due to the cause's nature, not the effect's need. The passage then addresses a comment by the author of Sharh al-Maqasid, which is refuted by clarifying that the original answer only negates essential need for a specific cause, not essential independence. Finally, the resolution is taken from al-Katibi: the effect essentially needs some cause, and its need for a specific cause is accidental, arising when that cause exists. If two independent causes coincide, each becomes specific and the effect would need each, restoring the problem; but if they alternate, no problem occurs because only the existing one is the cause. This sophisticated analysis addresses the logical consistency of the principle.

تلخيص النظر
عرض
استغناء
احتياج بالذات
تواردا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the answer's 'commitment' makes the theory inconsistent, because the effect seems to have no real cause.
Correction: The resolution shows that the effect's need for a specific cause is accidental, not essential, so no inconsistency arises. The effect always needs some cause essentially, but only accidentally depends on the specific one that actually gives it existence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Katibi's solution, when does the effect become in need of a specific cause?
A) By its essence
B) Accidentally when that cause exists and produces it
C) Only if two causes coincide
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Al-Katibi's solution resolves the problem by making the effect's need for a specific cause accidental, thereby preserving the principle that the effect needs some cause essentially.

?
The resolution hinges on alternation: only one cause at a time. In your daily struggles, do you recognize that Allah's actions and decrees alternate, and that your need is ultimately for Him alone? What would it mean for you to live with this consciousness?

Can a Single Effect Have Two Independent Causes?
States a problem and presents Imam al-Razi's answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's argument that a single individual can be caused by two independent causes without contradiction.

Core Idea
An effect can be independent of each cause's specificity yet caused by both.

This lesson addresses a classic problem in Islamic theology and philosophy: can one thing have two fully sufficient causes? Imam al-Razi offers a subtle solution by shifting the perspective from the effect's need to the cause's action. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping later debates on causality and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that the passage first states the impossibility claim, then presents Razi's counter-argument.
The phrase 'واحد بالشخص' means a single individual or particular entity.
2
Identify the problem
The problem is whether a single individual can be caused by two independent causes. The initial assumption is that it cannot, because it would require the effect to be dependent on two distinct causes, leading to contradiction.
Think about why two independent causes might conflict: each would claim full causality.
3
Understand Razi's solution
Razi argues that if the causation arises from the side of the cause (i.e., each cause requires that it be the cause), then the effect can be independent of the specificity of each cause while being caused by both. The effect does not need that particular cause; rather, each cause necessitates it.
Key: it is not necessary for the effect to need each cause specifically; the causal relation is on the cause's side.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how this argument preserves the possibility of multiple independent causes for a single effect without logical contradiction. This has implications for discussions of divine attributes and natural causality.
This is a sophisticated move in causal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الواحد بالشخص لا يعلل بعلتين مستقلتين يكون تعليلها بالمعينة ناشئاً من جانب العلة بأن تكون هذه المعينة تقتضي أن تكون علة لتلك الماهية، وتلك المعينة أيضاً تقتضي أن تكون علة لها، فهي مع استغنائها عن خصوصية كل منهما تكون معللة بهما كذا ذكره الإمام الرازي
The Fifth Observation - Second Objective: The one in person cannot be caused by two independent causes. Its being caused by a particular cause arises from the side of the cause, in that this particular cause requires that it be a cause for that essence, and that particular cause also requires that it be a cause for it. So it, while being independent of the specificity of each of them, is caused by both. This is what Imam al-Razi mentioned.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage states the problem and one proposed answer. The problem is whether a single individual (واحد بالشخص) can be caused by two independent causes. The answer presented is from Imam al-Razi: it is possible if the causal relation arises from the side of the cause, not from the effect's need. The effect does not need the specific cause; rather, each cause requires that it be the cause of that essence. So the effect is independent of the specificity of each cause, yet caused by both. This avoids the contradiction of being both needy and independent of each.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if an effect is caused by two independent causes, it must be dependent on each cause's specific nature.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the effect's independence from the specificity of each cause means it does not require that particular cause; the causality is on the cause's side. Thus, two independent causes can concur without the effect needing either specifically.

Checkpoint

D) The effect is completely independent of both causes.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi shows that a single individual can be caused by two independent causes if the causation is attributed to the cause's requirement, not the effect's need, thereby avoiding logical contradiction.

?
Razi's solution hinges on the cause's 'demand' to cause. Do you live as if your actions demand outcomes, or do you recognize the ultimate independence of Allah's creation? How does this affect your reliance on means versus reliance on Allah?

Al-Iji's Objection to al-Razi's Answer
Al-Iji objects that al-Razi's answer entails a commitment to the effect not needing its true cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Iji's objection that al-Razi's answer commits to the effect not needing its true cause specifically, and the implications for causality.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's answer saves free will but costs specific causation.

Al-Iji, the author of the Mawaqif, was not convinced by al-Razi's solution to the problem of two independent causes. He argues that if an effect does not need its cause specifically, then it doesn't really need that cause at all. This raises a profound problem: if causes are interchangeable, what makes a cause truly a cause? In this lesson, we examine al-Iji's objection and its logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Notice the parentheses — they are part of the text.
2
Identify the main claim of the objection
Al-Iji says the answer entails a 'commitment' (iltizam). Find what that commitment is: the effect does not need the cause specifically.
3
Follow the logical entailment
The passage argues: If the essence is caused by a particular cause only because that cause requires it (not because the essence needs it), then it is possible that the effect does not need what is truly its cause. This means the effect's need is only for some cause in general, not this specific cause.
This is the heart of the objection.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If al-Iji is right, does al-Razi's solution undermine the necessity of specific causes? How might al-Razi respond? Consider the role of divine causation and human free will.
This connects to broader debates in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المصنف : ( واعلم أن هذا الجواب فيه ( التزام ؛ لعدم احتياج المعلول إلى العلة بعينها)، مع كونها محتاجة إلى علة ما لا ،بعينها فإن الماهية إذا كانت معللة بعلة معينة لا لاحتياجها إليها، بل لاقتضاء تلك المعينة أن تكون علة للماهية، فقد جاز عدم احتياج المعلول إلى ما هو علة له حقيقة ( فلا يلزم احتياج الشخص المعلول للعلتين المستقلتين إلى كل منهما ) أي إلى شيء منهما بعينه، (بل) احتياجه إلى مفهوم أحدهما أي إلى علة ما، الذي لا ينافي الاجتماع)
The author said: (Know that this answer entails a commitment: because the effect does not need the cause specifically, while it needs some cause not specifically. For if the essence is caused by a particular cause not due to its need for it, but because that particular cause requires to be a cause for the essence, then it is permitted that the effect does not need what is truly its cause. (So the need of the personal effect for each of the two independent causes does not follow) i.e., for anything specifically from them, (but) its need is for the concept of one of them, i.e., for some cause, which does not contradict their combination).
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of al-Iji (the author of the Mawaqif) against Imam al-Razi's answer. He argues that the answer entails a commitment (التزام): if the effect does not need the specific cause, but only needs some cause in general, then it is possible that the effect does not need what is truly its cause. This shows that the effect's need for each independent cause specifically is not required; it only needs a cause in general. This does not lead to contradiction. The objection is that this undermines the notion of true causation.

Misconception: The objection completely rejects al-Razi's answer.
Correction: Al-Iji only points out an unwanted implication of al-Razi's answer, not a contradiction. The objection shows that al-Razi's answer commits to the effect not needing its cause specifically, which al-Iji finds problematic.

Clarifying the Objection: Causality and al-Katibi's Reply
Summarizes the objection, responds to Sharh al-Maqasid, and cites al-Katibi's solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection regarding whether an effect needs a particular cause, and how al-Katibi resolves the problem of two independent causes combining.

Core Idea
The effect needs any cause, not a specific one; specification is from the cause's side.

This passage addresses a crucial objection in discussions of causality: if an effect can be caused by either of two independent causes, does it need them both? The commentator clarifies the positions of Imam al-Razi and al-Katibi, showing that the effect's need is absolute, not particular, and that specification arises from the cause's side. This lesson unpacks these arguments and their resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main objection and responses
Look for the objection that an effect might be caused by two independent causes, and note how each authority (Imam al-Razi, the commentator, al-Katibi) responds.
3
Focus on Imam al-Razi's statement
Find Imam al-Razi's words about absolute need and specification. Understand that the effect needs some cause, but the particular cause is determined by the cause's side.
This is the foundation of the discussion.
4
Examine the reply to Sharh al-Maqasid
See how the commentator corrects Sharh al-Maqasid's misunderstanding: the negation is of essential need for a particular cause, not of essential independence.
5
Understand al-Katibi's solution
Al-Katibi introduces the distinction between essential and accidental dependence. Work through his example of two independent causes combining versus occurring successively.
This resolves the original objection except in the case of simultaneous causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتلخيص النظر : هي هي سره، لكن عبارة الإمام في المباحث صريحة في هذا المعنى حيث قال: فإن المعلول يحتاج إلى علة ما، ثم إن استناده إلى تلك المعينة بعينها ليس لأمر عائد إلى المعلول، بل لأن ذات العلة لما العلة مقتضية لذلك المعلول، فالحاجة المطلقة من جانب المعلول، وتعيين من جانبها . ولعل في قول الشارح قدس سره، كذا ذكره الإمام إشارة خفية إلى ما قلنا . قوله : (تكون معللة بهما والتعليل بهما لا يقتضي الاحتياج إليهما بخصوصهما، ولا يلزم اجتماع الاستغناء والاحتياج. قوله : ( إلى ما هو علة له حقيقة وهي المعينة، فإنها المعطية لوجودها، لا المطلقة. قوله : ( إلى شيء منهما ) أي ليس المراد رفع الإيجاب الكلي، كما هو المتبادر ، بل الـ الكلي، وهو ظاهر. قوله : (فهي مع استغنائها إلخ فيه رد على شارح المقاصد حيث قال في تلخيص هذا الجواب الذي نقل عن الإمام والحاصل أن الماهية النوعية بالنظر إلى ذاتها ليست محتاجة إلى العلة المعينة ولا غنية عنها، بل كل من ذلك بالعارض. ووجه الرد أن الذي ذكره الإمام في الجواب نفي احتياج الماهية النوعية بالذات إلى خصوصية كل من العلتين، لا نفي استغنائها بالذات عنها، وهو الظاهر. قوله: (وتلخيص النظر إلخ) الجواب عن هذا النظر مستفاد من كلام الكاتبي في شرح الملخص . حيث قال : المعلول بحسب الذات وإن لم يكن مفتقراً إلى هذه العلة المعينة، لكنه مفتقر إلى علة ما وتلك العلة المعينة لما وجدت وأوجدت المعلول عرض للمعلول الافتقار إليها. وتقرير هذا الجواب :هاهنا أن المعلول الشخصي إذا اجتمع عليه علتان مستقلتان تعين كل واحدة منهما، احتياج المعلول إلى نفسها على ما تقدم من أن تعين العلة من جانبها، فيلزم احتياج المعلول إلى كل واحدة منهما بعينها، ويعود المحذور. ولهذا إذا لم يجتمعا، بل تواردا لم يلزم محذور، إذ المتعين للعلية على تقدير وجود كل واحدة منهما إنما هو الموجود حينئذ،
And the summary of the view: Its secret is this. But the expression of Imam in al-Mabahith is explicit in this meaning, where he said: The effect needs some cause. Then its dependence on that particular cause specifically is not due to something returning to the effect, but because the essence of the cause, since the cause requires that effect. So absolute need is from the side of the effect, and specification from its side. Perhaps in the commentator's (may his secret be sanctified) statement 'thus Imam mentioned' is a subtle indication to what we said. His statement: 'It is caused by both, and being caused by them does not require need for them specifically, and there is no necessary combination of independence and need.' His statement: 'To what is truly its cause, which is the particular cause, for it gives existence to it, not the absolute cause.' His statement: 'To something from them' means the intent is not to remove the universal affirmer, as is immediately understood, but rather the universal, and it is apparent. His statement: 'So it with its independence etc.' contains a reply to the author of Sharh al-Maqasid, who said in summarizing this answer transmitted from Imam: The gist is that the specific essence, with respect to its essence, is neither in need of the particular cause nor independent of it; rather each of these is by accident. The aspect of the reply is that what Imam mentioned in the answer is the negation of the essential need of the specific essence for the specificity of each of the two causes, not the negation of its essential independence from them, and that is apparent. His statement: 'And the summary of the view etc.' The answer to this view is derived from the words of al-Katibi in Sharh al-Mulakhkhas, where he said: The effect, in terms of the essence, although not in need of this particular cause, is in need of some cause. And that particular cause, when it exists and brings about the effect, the effect becomes incidentally dependent on it. The formulation of this answer is: Here, if the personal effect has two independent causes combined, each of them becomes specified, and the effect's need for itself, as previously stated that the specification of the cause is from its side, then the need of the effect for each of them specifically follows, and the problem returns. For this reason, if they do not combine but occur successively, no problem arises, for the one specified for causality given the existence of each is only the one that exists at that time.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This long passage provides clarification and responses. It begins by summarizing the view (تلخيص النظر) and then presents Imam al-Razi's own words from al-Mabahith to show that the effect needs some cause absolutely, but the specification is from the cause. Then the commentator explains various glosses (قوله) on specific phrases, including a reply to the author of Sharh al-Maqasid who misinterpreted the answer. Finally, it cites al-Katibi's solution: the effect by essence needs some cause, but when a particular cause exists, the effect becomes accidentally dependent on it. However, if two independent causes combine, each becomes specified, leading back to the original problem. If they occur successively, no problem arises.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An effect that can be caused by two independent causes is independent of any specific cause and therefore needs no cause at all.
Correction: The effect always needs some cause; it is only accidentally dependent on a particular cause. Even with two possible causes, it needs at least one.

Checkpoint

B) The effect becomes needless of any cause

Key Takeaway
Al-Katibi resolves the problem by distinguishing essential need for any cause from accidental need for a particular cause; two independent causes coexisting reintroduce the problem, but successive occurrence avoids it.
